==PAGE:4==
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Globalization and modernization have led to the expansion of formerly omitted
areas like the Arctic. Governments and companies see a lot of potential in places
like Greenland or Svalbard with mining or tourist industries. The development of
these industries requires a lot of infrastructure. A unique aspect of soil’s behavior
in cold climates comes from its frozen state in negative temperature conditions for
a long time, called permafrost. Permafrost displays unique behavior upon applied
stress, a time-dependent deformation called creep. This effect is destructive and
undesirable in engineering design. Its behavior is complex, with many variables
influencing it, so studying its complexity and finding suitable models that can
simulate its behavior over time without time-consuming and expensive experiments
are crucial for the Arctic construction industry.
This thesis examines frozen ice-rich soil from Longyearbyen for classification,
properties determination, and observation of its mechanical behavior under stress,
simulating a structure to study time-dependent deformation behavior and choosing
a suitable model that can simulate deformation accurately through time. The
most accurate model is used for settlement predictions of two foundation systems
to compare their performance with precise measurements from the literature.
Considering the complexity of the creep effect, temperature dependence is examined
as well, based on the compressive strength of soil under different temperatures,
and how climate change might affect this is also discussed.
The study uses ISO tests to classify soil, determine its basic properties, and study
mechanical behavior with a series of uniaxial compression tests to identify examined
soil. The thesis describes the necessary theory and equations behind all tests and
properties and chosen models for creep simulation and foundation systems in the
literature review. It acknowledges knowledge gaps in the literature and encountered
constraints with used methods and available equipment.
UNIS EAST frozen soil was classified as gap-graded silty loam, low plasticity clay
with well bonded no excess ice cryostructure. Creep tests on samples resulted in
different states being reached, dependent on temperature and applied load. Results
determined that Ladanyi’s and Vyalov’s models were accurate enough to analyze
and compare them to experiments. They deduced Ladanyi’s model as the most
precise for long-term time-dependent deformation under load. The model was used
for two foundation systems that showed comparable results of settlements and
settlement rate prediction for specific foundation scenarios, compared to literature
sources, proving the model’s effectiveness. Compression strength results under
different temperatures proved its significant influence by giving a six times lower
value from -10◦C to -2◦C that decreases if the temperature rises.
The concluded results display an accurate model of the long-term creep effect, but
its considerable assumptions suggest further analysis of real scenarios and instan-
taneous strain determination. Additionally, proving high-temperature dependency
urges us to study climate change’s impact on long-term predictions for frozen soil
and methods that might mitigate this effect, like artificial cooling systems.
i
==PAGE:6==
Preface
This master thesis has been created to investigate the properties of the time-
dependent deformation behavior in permafrost soil under uniaxial compression tests
within different temperatures. Based on test results, two chosen models are picked
to use along with experiment data to find the one that most accurately assesses
the expected long-term time-dependent deformation for the studied Longyearbyen,
Svalbard location.
Choosing a geotechnical model aims to develop an accurate foundation behavior
prediction over time to ensure the stability of constructions in Longyearbyen. The
thesis also investigates the influence of climate change in the Svalbard region
on creep behavior in permafrost soil and whether it significantly affects overall
deformation behavior.
The idea behind using accurate models of creep behavior is to predict permafrost soil
deformation in the area without using more detailed and expensive investigation
methods.
The Arctic is a secluded and limited place where such research is
unavailable or time-consuming.
The thesis is the work of a Master’s student attending the UNIS University in
Svalbard Longyearbyen, who based the project on fieldwork and laboratory work
and conducted a data analysis, modeling, and foundation prediction scenarios that
led to results and concluded the necessary findings, needed for answering the raised
questions in the objectives.
UNIS University resolves the finances of the thesis, covering the expenses of
fieldwork in the form of a one-week drilling operation conducted by a private
company named Multiconsult, along with the author of this thesis. The university
also provides the necessary laboratory space and equipment, which is essential to
completing the experiments for this project.
Due to the thesis’s double degree profile from DTU and NTNU University and its
central location at UNIS University, it is being supervised by three different entities.
The supervisors’ names are mentioned on the title page, and the cooperation works
through weekly online meetings with the master student.
Raw data of uniaxial compression measurements in different temperatures is
available to download and use in the DataverseNO repository, an official research
repository of NTNU. The title of the dataset is: "Data of uniaxial compression in
different temperatures for frozen soil from Svalbard, Longyearbyen UNIS EAST
site" and its DOI: "https://doi.org/10.18710/RXIUHF"
Author:
Krystian Andrzej Czerniak

Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1
Project background
According to (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004), as well as universally recognized
nowadays in other scientific literature, permafrost can be defined as ground (soil or
rock) remaining below 0◦C for two consecutive years, which includes two straight
winters and the intervening summer. The presence of ice is not necessary to qualify
the ground as permafrost. Most permafrost on the planet is warmer than -5◦C,
which means that unfrozen water content is more significant than frozen water
content, based on (Williams and Smith, 1995).
In the recent article (Obu and Westermann, 2019), it is estimated, based on
measurements, that permafrost covers around 15% of the exposed surface land
area. The same article suggests, based on modeling, that permafrost covers up to
22% of the exposed surface land area. The modeling attempts to consider sporadic
and discontinuous permafrost patches that are difficult to detect with empirical
methods such as land exploration. The frequency of permafrost occurrence in the
northern parts of the world is very significant. It has a lot of influence on civil
engineering, so studying it is very important.
Figure 1.1.1: Illustration of an estimated permafrost map - probability calculated
as the fraction of model runs with MAGT below 0◦C
(Obu and Westermann, 2019)
1
==PAGE:24==
2
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
In 2017, the Arctic population was estimated at around 7 million inhabitants living
in over 1,500 settlements throughout the area. Approximately 60% of these Arctic
settlements are located in permafrost areas, of which 46% are coastal settlements,
and more than two-thirds of these 7 million inhabitants live in settlements with
permafrost areas (Jungsberg, Turunen, and Heleniak, 2019). All of this considered,
there is a lot of demand for permafrost study and research because it is a pervasive
geotechnical obstacle in Arctic foundation engineering.
Figure 1.1.2: Illustration of Arctic settlements located on permafrost or outside.
(Jungsberg, Turunen, and Heleniak, 2019)
Permafrost properties like strength or deformation behavior are highly temperature-
dependent. Other essential characteristics influencing it are particle size and ice
content because more significant ice bonds increase soil strength (Berggren, 1983).
The loss of frozen water content over unfrozen water content in permafrost due to
temperature increases significantly changes strength and deformation. Permafrost
with low ice saturation is called ice-poor or no-ice soil, whereas permafrost, which
contains significant amounts of ice in the form of ice lenses or ice layers, is called
ice-rich permafrost.
Permafrost’s thermal properties are especially relevant to this study because of
the warming effect taking place in the Arctic, which is indicated by an increase in
air temperature, humidity, precipitation, river discharge, wildfire, as well as the
decline of snow cover, sea ice extent, and volume in the last 47 years (Box and
2
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
3
Colgan, 2019). These impact the mechanical properties of the frozen ground soil,
resulting in construction difficulties where preserving or building on permafrost gets
increasingly more expensive and burdensome due to mechanical deterioration. The
specific and potentially variable permafrost properties demand a lot of observation
and research to create a safe environment for Arctic infrastructure development.
1.2
Project Motivation
The Arctic exploration and expansion are growing with new infrastructure and
population.
As technology and globalization progress, interest in the Arctic
scientific circle increases, which consists of observing and experimenting on the
Arctic climate, geology, biology, and many more scientific fields. According to the
official website of statistics of Norway (Statistics-Sentralbyra, 2013), the population
and inhabitants of Svalbard keep growing each year. This growth creates a demand
for infrastructure development, a real challenge due to unusual conditions that do
not usually occur in other parts of the world. One of these challenges is the soil
condition, which deals with an additional frozen water content created by a cold
climate. This specific soil condition adds an ice influence to the ground’s mechanical
properties that can potentially help or hinder the structure stability, depending on
the engineering approach applied to the soil (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
One of the most essential effects of permafrost, considering its deformation, is
creep. Creep is essentially an effect of time-dependent deformation due to the
load acting on soil (Williams and Smith, 1995). The effect is caused by the ice
particle behavior, which displays a viscous type of deformation, meaning that
under constant stress, the deformation is not instant but deforms over a long
period. If the time-dependent deformation prediction is not made for frozen soil,
the structure designed to withstand the load at the beginning of the structure’s life
may fail after a few years. This risk is why studying this deformation is necessary
to predict the soil’s behavior and predicted settlement after long-term constant
load influence (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
The motivation behind this project is to increase the knowledge of the time-
dependent deformation process in permafrost soil in the Longyearbyen Svalbard
area for the duration of the structure’s "lifespan." The demand for infrastructure
in that place keeps growing, and assessing time-dependent behavior, especially
for extended periods considering warming effects, is very important for future
development. The knowledge of long-term time-dependent behavior is minimal, as
the Arctic is a unique place with limited resources and research. Still, due to the
significance and relevance of permafrost in inhabited areas, there is an increase in
demand for a better understanding of this process.
1.3
Thesis structure
The thesis project consists of seven chapters. Each chapter, except for the first,
third, and last one, ends with internal conclusions and a summary to highlight the
most critical information established in it.
3
==PAGE:26==
4
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
The first chapter presents the background of this thesis, considering permafrost’s
influence and relevance on humans. It also provides information about the thesis’s
motivation and overall structure. As an introduction, the chapter does not need to
include any conclusions.
The second chapter consists of a literature study that describes frozen soil and its
properties, which this thesis will designate, along with laboratory methods used
to determine them. The chapter also introduces the concepts of time-dependent
modeling and describes six unique but commonly used models for frozen soil time-
dependent deformation assessment. The chapter gathers essential knowledge about
the potential influence of climate change on the results, as long-term predictions
should consider this effect.
Next, the chapter expresses two universally used
foundation systems in the Arctic that are common in engineering design. In the
end, the chapter focuses on all possible knowledge gaps that were encountered
in relevance to this project, and because of them, some information may not be
described extensively.
The third chapter establishes the objectives, which, based on fieldwork, data
analysis, and modeling, need to be eventually answered and fulfilled in the overall
conclusions. The chapter also includes general information about the methodology
used to complete said objectives.
The fourth chapter focuses on all of the experimental efforts made for this thesis. It
starts with a description of the studied location and used frozen soil, describes the
fieldwork effort of sample collection, and establishes the thermal conditions in situ
for the tested location of frozen soil. Next, the chapter describes the laboratory
space and equipment necessary for the experiments and reports the exact test
procedures for these tests. Next, the chapter reflects on all of the constraints that
the experimental effort encountered, either with the equipment or methods used.
After that, the chapter explains and displays the tests’ results and makes necessary
conclusions for the soil assessment.
The fifth chapter focuses on theoretical time-dependent modeling, which, based on
the literature review and experimental effort, chooses two out of six models from
the review that are the most relevant for this case. Later, it denotes the necessary
models’ parameters based on experimental time-dependent deformation results
in the previous chapter. In the end, the chapter creates the results of modeled
deformation behavior, compares the results with the experiment, makes conclusions
about the accuracy and inaccuracy of used models, and depicts the one that is
more accurate for the examined frozen soil in the thesis.
The sixth chapter predicts foundation settlements and settlement rates for two
Arctic foundation systems using the chosen model from the previous chapter. It
compares the results to data from other sources displaying observed and documented
foundation time-dependent deformations.
The seventh chapter summarizes the project’s findings. It refers to the most critical
and relevant information established and, based on it, demonstrates conclusions
that answer and complete the objectives from the third chapter. The chapter ends
with suggestions for future work based on this project to either use or prove its
accuracy and increase the already established knowledge from this thesis.
4
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 2
Literature review
2.1
Frozen soil
Frozen soil can be divided into two essential parts based on its thermal condition.
The surface of frozen soil, due to the seasonal change of air temperature, heat
flux, and weather conditions, does not keep the negative temperature all year.
It changes during the summer season into positive values, while the sun’s heat
flux penetrates the ground into a certain depth, at which the soil stops being
influenced by it and does not change anymore that much, up until the point where
the internal earth heat flux comes into place (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004). The
upper layer of frozen soil, where the temperature changes from positive to negative
during seasons, is called an active layer. The constant thawing and freezing of
the ground in that layer causes a lot of different frost activities, which influence
the ground’s properties and topography. The active layer’s thickness is mainly
influenced by the mean annual surface temperature(Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
The layer below the active layer in frozen soil does not exceed 0◦C throughout
the year, and it is called permafrost. This layer is significant in the Arctic regions
as it can extend to tens or even hundreds of meters below the surface (Williams
and Smith, 1995). Its thickness depends on the mean air, surface temperatures,
and the earth’s interior heat flux for a given local geothermal gradient and soil
properties. The earth’s interior heat flux increases in depth, which means that
sometimes permafrost can span hundreds of meters deep before it is warmed by the
earth’s interior heat flux enough to reach a favorable temperature where the ground
becomes unfrozen. Figure 2.1.1 shows a standard temperature profile for frozen soil
that shows the annual minimum, maximum, and mean temperatures for a given
depth underground. These temperature profiles for frozen soil are typically called
"trumpet curves" due to their unique shape, where the minimum and maximum
temperatures expand into opposite directions on the top (indicating the different
temperatures during seasonal changes for the top layers of the soil), creating a
trumpet-like shape. The point where the difference between a minimum and a
maximum temperature equals zero is called the depth of zero annual amplitude
(Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
5
==PAGE:28==
6
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
Figure 2.1.1: A schematic of the temperature profile for the frozen soil.
The most recent definition of permafrost technically does not specify the physical
state of the soil. This definition implies the frozen state, referring to the temperature
below 0◦C. Still, some soil conditions influence the freezing point of water and
can cause the freezing point to be below 0◦C. This effect is called a freezing point
depression, and the most common characteristic influencing it is salinity or pressure
(Shah and Mir, 2022). Figure 2.1.1 shows that the freezing point depression varies
in depth (as salinity and pressure influence it) and changes the thawing point of
soil, creating a layer known as seasonally active permafrost (transition zone) where
the soil is under 0◦C but it is still considered unfrozen.
In the Arctic, it is necessary to build structures on permafrost due to its commonness
and vastness. The structure’s weight puts pressure on the ground and results in a
specific stress over an area, affecting the frozen soil below. These stresses result in
the development of ground deformation. The structure’s deformation happens at its
foundation level, usually in a downward vertical manner (F.McCarthy, 2007) where
stress is mainly vertical with a minor influence of other directions (F.McCarthy,
2007).
The mechanical difference between frozen and unfrozen soil is significant to the point
that it is easier to compare or treat the frozen soil similarly to ice (Andersland and
Ladanyi, 2004) or concrete (Berggren, 1983) from a mechanical point of view. This
difference is due to the ice cementation significantly increasing the soil’s strength
and stiffness because of an increased cohesion force between ice crystals and soil
particles (Williams and Smith, 1995). Frozen soil, upon loading, experiences a
time-dependent deformation called creep (Williams and Smith, 1995).
6
==PAGE:29==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
7
2.1.1
Classification and Index properties
We can identify soil as unconsolidated sediments and deposits of solid particles from
rock disintegration. For engineering purposes, it is often essential to include the
residue of bio components such as plants, animal-origin particles, or even human-
buried trash and industrial waste (F.McCarthy, 2007). Soil mass accumulates
individual particles that are bonded with each other by mechanical or attractive
means. This bond comes from the water-filled voids between particles. When
the soil has a negative temperature, part of the water inside the voids freezes,
creating ice bonds between rock particles and ice crystals that cement and solidify
the soil, creating a stronger bond between particles (Williams and Smith, 1995).
The freezing water expands, resulting in particle separation and a volume increase
(Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
In geotechnical engineering, soil conditions and properties are critical to study.
As a naturally created material, the ground’s matrix is unique and irregular as
a rule, and it is impossible to have the same one twice, which is why it is so
essential to perform soil’s parametrization and identification (F.McCarthy, 2007).
Various in-situ and laboratory tests can determine the soil’s properties that are
responsible for its mechanical behavior. As a result of the broad scope of soil
variations, some of them have different properties than others, which is why
engineers have established universal index properties and soil classification systems
that determine the type and category of soil (F.McCarthy, 2007). In Europe, a
universal system for this classification has been established by an international
organization of standardization called ISO 14688 (Geotechnical Investigation and
Testing - Identification and Classification of Soil).
Frozen soil classification is divided into three separate parts. The first part identifies
the soil’s phase without the ice inclusion using the unified soil classification system
(USCS). USCS categorizes soils into granular, cohesionless, highly organic soils, or
fine-grained cohesive soils. Different categories in this part correspond to separate
mechanical behavior (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004). The second part considers
additional characteristics of ice inclusion, like frozen water content. The third part
describes the ice matrix, which is grouped into three main categories. Soil with
segregated ice that is not visible to the unaided eye (marked as N), soils where the
segregated ice is visible (marked as V), and the last category describes the soil,
which is only a tiny percentage of the matrix, where the rest of it is ice (marked as
ICE) (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
The N-type frozen soil, upon close inspection, will reveal the presence of ice inside
the pores through a crystalline reflection and a sheen on trimmed or fractured
surfaces. The N-type is divided into poorly bonded, fragile frozen soil (Nf), and
well-bonded frozen soil (Nb). The well-bonded type has two subgroups, which
reveal either the absence of excess ice in a frozen soil (Nbn) or its presence (Nbe)
upon thawing. It essentially means that the (Nbn) type is not fully saturated when
frozen, whereas the (Nbe) type is (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
The V-type frozen soil has four subgroups based on the sample’s visible formations
of excess ice. Figure 2.1.2 shows those formations, which are the individual ice
crystals (Vx), the ice coatings on particles which is similar to the first subgroup
7
==PAGE:30==
8
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
but have larger patches of excess ice (Vc), the random or irregularly oriented ice
formations in the form of thin layers (Vr), and the oriented ice formations (Vs)
(Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
Frozen soil is classified as ICE when the ice formations are more significant than
25 mm thick. Two subgroups of ICE are with the soil inclusions (ICE + soil type)
and no inclusions (ICE). It isn’t easy to distinguish those subgroups in a frozen
state, so the soil is thawed and then has its volume of water and soil measured
where the percentage of soil in the sample determines the subgroup (Andersland
and Ladanyi, 2004).
Figure 2.1.2: Examples of frozen soil classes for ice matrix determination with
non-visible ice (a), visible ice less than 25 mm thick (b), and visible ice with more
than 25 mm thick (c).
8
==PAGE:31==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
9
2.1.1.1
Structural components of frozen soil
Frozen soil consists of ice, solid particles, unfrozen water, and gas. It is called a
four-phase system that changes into a three-phase system upon complete thawing.
The solid particles are different fractions of minerals and organic matter arranged
among themselves, creating voids. These voids are filled with various proportions
and arrangements of ice, unfrozen water, and gas. Figure 2.1.3 shows the mass
and volume relationship schematic for frozen and unfrozen soil phase systems
(Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
Figure 2.1.3: Four-phase and three-phase systems of mass and volume relation-
ships for the frozen and unfrozen soil.
Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004
Frozen soil’s phase relationship allows for defining some parameters:
- Void ratio e
e = Vv

Dividing the volume of voids (Vv) by the volume of solids (Vs) or using solid density
(ρs) and dry density (ρd) as shown in the equation 2.1, gives the void ratio. The
void ratio is a decimal value, where the lower value suggests fewer voids inside the
soil (F.McCarthy, 2007).
- Porosity n
n = Vv
V · 100% =
e
1 + e · 100%
(2.2)
Porosity is a ratio of the volume of voids (Vv) and the total volume of soil (V ) in
percentage. It is related to the void ratio by an equation 2.2. The lower porosity
value also means fewer voids in the soil (F.McCarthy, 2007).

- Water content w

Water content in frozen soil plays an essential part due to the nature of water
behavior upon thawing and freezing. The water, upon freezing, expands the soil,
which leads to structural change. The magnitude of these changes depends on the
water content (Williams and Smith, 1995). In unfrozen soil, it is calculated by
dividing the mass of water (Mw) over the mass of solid particles (Ms), but in the
four-phase system, water mass is a sum of the ice mass (Mi) and unfrozen water
mass (Muw). For frozen soil, water content consists of unfrozen water content (wu)
and ice content (wi).
w = wu + wi
(2.4)

Bulk density is the density of the total soil mass in bulk volume. It is influenced by
water, ice, air, soil proportions, and soil density (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).

The density of soil particles ranges between 2.5 and 2.8 g/cm3 because the particles
are made of stone and minerals with similar densities. The central changing
fact is the voids within the particles. Usually, the particle density is higher for
smaller particles than the larger ones because the larger ones are more expanded
(F.McCarthy, 2007). In soil engineering literature, the commonly assumed value
for sand density is 2.65 g/cm3 and 2.7 g/cm3 for clay.

Excess ice is a percentile of additional ice volume in the saturated soil. It is
obtained by thawing the frozen soil and measuring the volume of supernatant
water (Wv), as well as the volume of saturated sediment (Sv) and using equation
2.8 where 1.09 is a coefficient to estimate the equivalent ice volume.
2.1.1.2
Visual and tactile inspection in situ
The initial visual and tactile inspection of soil immediately after extraction may be
considered the first and fundamental soil analysis, especially for frozen soil, since
the thermal regime of frozen soil is essential (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004). This
inspection can reveal information about the state of soil underground temperature,
its type, and any unusual or unique changes in the matrix. It is visually possible
to determine whether the soil is dominant in gravel or the sand fraction. Tactile
inspection can determine particle size by rubbing the soil between an index finger
and thumb. If the feeling is coarse and grainy can be felt, it may suggest a
dominant sand fraction. If the feeling is rough but not coarse enough to feel
individual grains, it can mean a predominantly silty fraction. If the soil feels
slightly coarse, it combines clay and silt. Lastly, if the rubbing soil feels smooth, it
may be a dominant clay fraction.
2.1.1.3
Particle size distribution
Particle size distribution is a classification test that determines the percentages
of particles of different sizes inside the soil. In this type of test, we divide the
particles into four distinct types.
Table 2.1.1: Soil type, based on the particle size.
Soil type
Particle size range
Gravel
20 mm to 4.75 mm
Sand
4.75 mm to 0.075 mm
Silt
0.075 mm to 0.002 mm
Clay
less than 0.002 mm
After determining the percentages of all four types of particle sizes, a textural
classification of soil is used to determine the soil class.
11
==PAGE:34==
12
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
Figure 2.1.4: Chart for the textural classification of soil based on silt, clay, and
sand percentage.
Particle size distribution test is not only essential to determine the dominant
particle size, which has a significant influence on the type of soil properties, but
also its distribution can give information about the soil’s origin and method of
deposition (F.McCarthy, 2007).
Figure 2.1.5: Shapes for particle size distribution curve expected for well-graded
soil (upper left), uniform soil (upper right), gap-graded soil (lower left), and young
residual soil (lower right).
Figure 2.1.5 shows that a well-graded soil produces a straight long curve, meaning
that there is a distribution over a large range of sizes. The uniform soil has the
most particles of a similar size, with an almost vertical curve, and does not range
that much. A gap-graded soil experiences an absence of intermediate sizes along
its matrix.
12
==PAGE:35==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
13
Figure 2.1.6: Shapes for particle size distribution curve expected for intermediate
maturing soil (upper left), fully maturing soil (upper right), glacial soil (lower left),
glacial-alluvial soil (lower right)
Particle size distribution indicates the soil’s origin as well. Based on the formation
process, two categories are transported and residual soils. Residual soils are created
from withering rock and organic materials that remain at the location of their
origin. In contrast, the transported soils are moved from their origin, caused by an
outside influence such as currents or glaciers (F.McCarthy, 2007). Figures 2.1.5
and 2.1.6 show young residual, intermediate maturing, and fully maturing soil,
which are different stages of residual soils because they change in time due to
particle breakage. Glacial and glacial-alluvial soil from figure 2.1.6 forms because of
particle transportation. The transportation process conditions the transportation
of specific fractions and may be inefficient in certain sizes, influencing particle size
distribution (F.McCarthy, 2007).
Particle size distribution is described by parameters correlated to specific diameters
in correlation to their percentages of the whole soil. For example, the adequate
size of sand is a particle size corresponding to the 10 percent passing size from
the grain-size curve (D10). This size also corresponds to the permeability and
capillarity of the soil. The effective sizes of certain percentile of total volume give
information about particle size distribution’s curvature (Cc), uniformity (Cu), and
sorting coefficient (So) (F.McCarthy, 2007). These coefficients can be calculated
using equations 2.9, 2.10, and 2.11 accordingly.
Cz = (D30)2
D10D60
(2.9)
Cu = D60
D10
(2.10)
So =
r
D75
D25
(2.11)
D30 and D60 are analogical to the D10 but they correspond to both 60% and
30% passing by weight. The same goes with D75 and D25, which correspond to
diameters of 75% and 25% of residue passing. Well-graded soils typically have a
Cu greater than 10, whereas uniform soils have a Cu of less than 5. For the Cc,
13
==PAGE:36==
14
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
a value between 1 and 3 is the most common with well-graded soil (Andersland
and Ladanyi, 2004). Poorly sorted soils are well-graded and have sufficiently larger
values of sorting coefficient (So).
The specific surface area of particles is related to the unfrozen water content
at a given temperature. Therefore, particle size influences the water-ice phase
relationship. The particle surface and ice bond are interrupted by a thin layer
of unfrozen water, and the amount is more considerable if the particle surface is
bigger (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
2.1.1.4
Cryostructure
Cryostructure is a pattern of ice inclusions within frozen soil that depend on
deposition and cryogenic genesis. A closed system characterizes it and highly
depends on the freezing conditions. Rapid freezing creates a large cryostructure
with visible ice in the pores. In contrast, slow freezing at -1◦C allows the moisture
to be redistributed in the structure and depends on both the physio-chemical
characteristics of sediments and the direction of freezing. The result of moisture
redistribution is the formation of segregated ice patterns (Bray, 2008).
Cryostructure influences both properties and deformation behavior. The exact and
detailed effects of the cryostructure on the mechanical properties and creep behavior
of frozen soils have still not been explored that much. Perhaps the most significant
and detailed information can be derived from (Pekarskaya, 1965), where the author
looks at the effect of cryostructure on the frozen soil deformation behavior. Also,
(Vialov, 1959) and (Vialov and Dokuckayev, 1980) expand the discussion on the
impact of cryostructure. Apart from those sources, little significant literature
addresses the role of cryostructure in the geotechnics of frozen soils.
The cryostructure of frozen soil is classified in terms of time formation relative to
the deposition of the host material, which splits permafrost into epigenetic and
syngenetic type (French and Shur, 2010). Epigenetic permafrost is created after
the host material was formed after a time lag of thousands or even millions of years.
Syngenetic permafrost is proportional to the sedimentation rate and characterizes
cold climate landscapes influenced by relatively continuous deposition (Gilbert,
Kanevskiy, and Murton, 2016). It is also possible to differentiate quasi-syngenetic
permafrost, which forms the top layer of the permafrost by upward freezing. Also,
many permafrost landscapes consist of quasi-syngenetic, syngenetic, and epigenetic
permafrost, making them polygenetic type (Gilbert, Kanevskiy, and Murton, 2016).
An analysis of cryostructure spatial distribution and the nature of ice determines
the distinction between them.
Different cryostructures of frozen soil are determined and distinguished by the
amount, shape, scale, and distribution of ice inclusions in the form of pores
and segregated ice within the frozen sediment (French and Shur, 2010). The
Classification of cryostructures is not yet unified, and changes have been made
in different literature sources. For example, one Russian Classification mentions
18 different cryostructures (Pekarskaya, 1965), while the other one is 14 (Vialov
and Dokuckayev, 1980). They are, however, complex and unwieldy to a certain
extent (French and Shur, 2010) because they cannot be specified without a complex
14
==PAGE:37==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
15
analysis. American studies such as (Bray, 2012), (Gilbert, Kanevskiy, and Murton,
2016), and (French and Shur, 2010) suggest another classification for cryostructure
that is more intuitive and easier to classify. They also observe trends in the
occurrence of certain cryostructures based on formation time.
Cryogenic structures of syngenetic permafrost appear to be rhythmically organized
(layered and lenticular) because they reflect the accumulation effects of deposition
surfaces subjected to cold-climate conditions. When permafrost rises through the
sediment, the depth zone located within the ice formation zone increases (French
and Shur, 2010). Two relevant and widely used cryostructure classifications exist
in the Arctic, especially in Svalbard. The first one is proposed by (Murton and
French, 1994) and encompasses a range of main cryostructures in permafrost.
Figure 2.1.7: Classification of cryostructures, showing types and codes (A) and
subgroups for layered and lenticular types (B), where white parts represent ice and
black ones the sediment.
Murton and French, 1994
In part A of figure 2.1.7, the structureless and reticulate cryostructures are charac-
teristic of epigenetic permafrost, while layered and lenticular are characteristic of
syngenetic permafrost (Murton and French, 1994).
The second reliable cryostructure classification is suggested by (Bray, 2012). This
Classification is more detailed and requires special methods in analysis, such as
micro and macro scale images taken with an environmental scanning electron
microscope (ESEM) because it focuses on cryostructures with non-visible ice (N).
15
==PAGE:38==
16
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
Figure 2.1.8: Macro and micro images made by ESEM of different soil cryostruc-
tures. Scale of micro images is 250 µm
Bray, 2012
In figure 2.1.8, images A and B show a micro-lenticular cryostructure consisting of
thin lenticular-shaped lenses that saturate the soil. Their thickness is less than 0.5
mm and around 4-10 mm long. Microscopic analysis indicates that soil particles
are gathered inside of ice matrix (Bray, 2012). Compression tests on soil with this
type of cryostructure indicated a significance of orientation of lenses, which is why
this type is divided into horizontal micro-lenticular (HML) where ice lenses are
perpendicular to the applied stress and vertical micro-lenticular (VML) where the
applied stress is parallel to the ice lenses (Bray, 2012).
Images C and D show an undistributed massive cryostructure that is silt cemented
together without any visible segregated ice. ESEM analysis indicates ice existing
in pores, meaning over-saturated soil conditions when it was frozen (Bray, 2012).
Images E and F indicate a remolded soil with massive cryostructure (RM) consistent
in the layout of ice and soil particles between each other in a reticulate manner
(Bray, 2012).
Images G and H present the reticulate-chaotic cryostructure (RC) characterized
by randomly oriented ice lenses between 1-5 mm thick and 1-5 cm, making them
potentially visible. The silt structure between ice lenses is massive and consolidated
(Bray, 2012).
