2.1.1.5
Salinity and freezing point depression
The water inside the pores of soils contains dissolved salts. The mass of salt present
in the soil, divided by the mass of soil, is called salinity, and it is represented by
a promille or a percentage as a value (F.McCarthy, 2007). The most essential
characteristic of salinity is that it influences the freezing and thawing index of water
solution. Therefore, it is a significant characteristic in frozen soil as it changes
the water and ice conditions. Salinity increase causes ice reduction in frozen soil,
causing a decrease in strength and creep rate increase (Andersland and Ladanyi,
2004).
Articles such as (Kotov and Agapin, 2021) and (Ding and Hu, 2022) show that
increasing salinity causes an increase of unfrozen water content with higher salinity
leading to loss of ice cementation and strength decrease. Figure 2.1.9 shows the
relation between unfrozen water content and salinity in 2 types of soils of different
temperatures.
Figure 2.1.9: Unfrozen water content wu as a function of salinity Sn for sands
(1) and lean clays (2) for -2◦C (a), -4◦C (b) and -10◦C (c)
Aleksyutina and Motenko, 2016
17
==PAGE:40==
18
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
The presence of salt in water changes its freezing temperature, which decreases
when salinity is higher. The effect, where water’s freezing point decreases, is called
freezing point depression. It does not necessarily happen due to salinity. Pressure
and suction can also influence the freezing and thawing index of soil. Pressure and
suction are less significant than salinity in an environment close to atmospheric
pressure (Williams and Smith, 1995).
Figure 2.1.10: Freezing point for different compositions of salt and water.
Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004
Figure 2.1.10 shows that different composition of water and salt decreases temper-
ature when the solution has a higher percentage of NaCl. For example, in point A,
a solution of 3.5% NaCl and 96.5% water starts to freeze around -2.1◦C, leading to
ice crystals forming in brine (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004). In point B, where
the composition of 12% salt and 88% water, the liquid starts to freeze at around
-8◦C. The equation describing the change of freezing temperature due to salinity is
written as follows:
Tfpd = Tref ·
Sn
100 + Sn
(2.12)
In equation 2.12, Tfpd is a new freezing point temperature, Sn is salinity in %, and
Tref is a reference temperature, established as 57◦C for sea salt or 62◦C for NaCl.
In figure 2.1.10, point C, where the solution of salt and water is still liquid in the
lowest temperature possible, which is -21◦C at around 23.3% of salinity, is called
the eutectic composition (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
Sometimes, freezing point depression is not a low temperature enough to start
the freezing process because soil water is located within closed pores. This effect
requires the pore water to reach the supercooled temperature Tsc below the discussed
freezing point, established by freezing point depression (Andersland and Ladanyi,
2004).
18
==PAGE:41==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
19
Figure 2.1.11: Standard cooling curve of frozen soil.
Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004
The pore water starts to freeze only when subjected to a supercooled temperature
Tsc or lower. At that point, the water is in a metastable equilibrium state when
the sudden transformation of free water to ice is triggered by nucleation centers,
which are the aggregation of water and soil molecules. When the ice forms that
way, it releases latent heat that causes the temperature to rise to the freezing point.
From now on, the free water freezes at the Tf, up to an end when the release of
latent heat slows the rate of cooling to a Te, which is shown in Figure 2.1.11. At
that point, all of the free water and most of the bound water, which is unfrozen
water film on the soil particles, is frozen at around -70◦C (Andersland and Ladanyi,
2004).
2.1.1.6
Atterberg limits
Fine-grained soil displays plastic and cohesive properties, whereas a lump of soil
can change shape without the volume change or any breakup. These properties
depend on the fines’ amount and mineralogy and the amount of water inside the
soil (Barnes, 2010). Upon the increase of water, the soil gets stickier and softer to
the point of losing shape, which is described as a liquid state of the soil—further
growth of water results in less interaction between soil particles and the creation
of the suspension. If the amount of water is decreased instead, it becomes stiffer
to the point of losing cohesive properties, and the soil becomes fragile and breaks
up. This state is called the semi-solid or semi-plastic solid.
19
==PAGE:42==
20
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
Figure 2.1.12: Atterberg limits with consistency descriptions according to BS
EN ISO 14688-1:2002.
In figure 2.1.12, the transitions between four primary states are defined as shrinkage
limit (SL), plastic limit (PL), and liquid limit (LL). The difference between the
liquid and plastic limits is called the plasticity index (PI) and is used to denote
the degree of soil plasticity (Barnes, 2010).
Figure 2.1.13: Plasticity chart, according to BS 5930:2015. Soils above the "A
line" are clays (C), and below are silts (M).
Barnes, 2010
Figure 2.1.13 shows a developed plasticity chart by Casagrande in 1948, showing
that clay soils lie above the A-line while silts and organic soils are below. The
equation for the plasticity index is given as follows:
PI = |LL −PL|
(2.13)
20
==PAGE:43==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
21
Another important parameter, denoted from Atterberg limits, is the liquidity index
(LI). It is a good indicator of where the natural moisture content lies concerning
Atterberg limits. It is defined as:
LI = w −PL
PI
(2.14)
Based on equation 2.14, when water content w is equal to the liquid limit, the
liquidity index equals 1, and when w = PL, it equals 0. This result means that
the value of the liquidity index in the natural soil within the plastic state varies
between 0 and 1. The natural deposit, where water content is greater or equal to
its liquid limit, will have a liquidity index greater than 1. Those types of soils are
called sensitive clays, as they can be stable in undisturbed conditions. Still, when
they are disturbed, they can immediately transform into a liquid state (Braja,
2008).
Atterberg limits are affected not only by the amount of water and mineralogy
but also by the number of clay particles in the soil because the plastic properties
of soil are caused by the absorbed water surrounding the clay particles. The
plasticity index linearly increases with the percent of clay fraction (Braja, 2008).
The correlation between the plasticity index and clay fraction varies in different
soils due to the type of clay minerals in each soil. In 1953, Skempton defined the
activity parameter as the line slope correlating the plasticity index and percentage
of clay minerals in soil (Braja, 2008). Equation 2.15 shows how to calculate activity,
and table 2.1.3 has the descriptions for different values.
A =
PI
percentage of clay fraction by weight
(2.15)
Table 2.1.3: Description of different values for activity
Description
Activity
Inactive
< 0.75
Normal
0.75 - 1.25
Active
1.25 - 2.0
Highly active
> 2.0
2.1.2
Mechanical properties
Generally, the overall strength of frozen soils is considered to consist of ice matrix
cohesion and frictional resistance of soil particles (Williams and Smith, 1995). It
is more potent than unfrozen soil and sometimes even than ice, and it displays
behavior similar to that of under-stress ice, which is a time-dependent creep
deformation. Its addition of ice is what mainly distinguishes it from unfrozen soil.
The only type of ice inside soil pores is normal hexagonal ice type 1h, one-phase
ice with a hexagonal crystal lattice structure that is the most common form of
frozen water in the biosphere. It is characterized by lower density than water, its
hardness, and transparency, which are crucial for forming hexagonal snowflakes
(Sanderson, 1983).
21
==PAGE:44==
22
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
Unfrozen water inside frozen soil exists: firmly bound (bound water) and weakly
bound (free water). Firmly bound water surrounds mineral particles, held by
high intermolecular forces that suppress freezing to a certain degree, making them
harder to freeze. Weakly bound water is the rest in pores that freeze more easily.
Due to the nature of firmly bound water, the unfrozen water content at a given
temperature is correlated with a specific area of mineral particles (Anderson and
Tice, 1972).
The complexity of frozen soil mechanics comes from the multiphase system of soil
particles, ice, unfrozen water, and air, shown in figure 2.1.14. The thermodynamic
conditions between components are equally crucial between each other because
temperature greatly influences ice behavior but also the amount of unfrozen water
conditioning ice content and cohesion from ice-particle cementation (Williams and
Smith, 1995).
Figure 2.1.14: Frozen soil model with its structural elements.
Figure 2.1.14 shows how complex and temperature-susceptible frozen soil is re-
garding mechanical behavior. Frozen soil’s behavior closely resembles ice, but
the unfrozen water and soil particle addition might lead to unexpected and more
complicated results for its behavior (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
2.1.2.1
Creep
Upon loading frozen soil, it is expected to experience an immediate deformation
(elastic and plastic) at first, and then time-dependent deformation consisting
mainly of creep, which is essentially time-dependent viscous deformation, where
various processes due to load acting on soil, are happening (Williams and Smith,
1995) and (Bray, 2008). Its scale and rate depend on time, ice content, water
content, cryostructure, stress, strain rates, soil composition, and other variables
that influence these properties, such as ice content, salinity, and pressure (Berggren,
1983).
22
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
23
Creep is split into three periods based on its rate behavior. The first period is when
the creep rate decreases, the second is when the rate remains constant, and the
third is when the creep rate increases. They are named primary, secondary, and
tertiary creeps accordingly (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004). Figure 2.1.15 shows
all three stages of creep, called creep curves, when the creep rate is decreasing (I),
is constant (II), and is increasing (III).
Figure 2.1.15: Illustrations showing creep curve in 3 different stages for strain
in time (Upper left), for strain rate in time (Upper right), and possible creep
responses with dominant states
When the load is initially applied to the soil, it may or may not develop an initial
elastic or instantaneous strain (ϵ0). After that, it proceeds into the primary creep
state (Bray, 2008). Some soils, especially ice-poor soils with little ice content, tend
not to proceed beyond the primary creep state. If the creep rate stops decreasing
and keeps a constant value, the soil enters the secondary creep, which is usually
the most dominant in ice-rich soils (Bray, 2008) and (Berggren, 1983). After some
time for higher stress values, the creep rate increases and goes into the tertiary
state, quickly leading to failure. In a high constant stress environment (significantly
exceeding the soil’s strength), deformation skips the secondary state and reaches
the tertiary after the primary state. In low constant stresses, secondary and tertiary
states may not happen at all (Bray, 2008).
2.1.2.2
Strength
Subjecting the mass or body of soil to external load causes the variation of normal
and shear stresses. Normal stress is acting perpendicular to the soil’s surface or an
axis of the object it is applied to, causing the potential reduction in size with its
direction of action towards the object (compression), or if the direction of action is
23
==PAGE:46==
24
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
the opposite, it causes an extension (tension). Shear stresses act along the surface
or axis of the object. The ability to support stress to a certain point, imposed by
the size of the external load, before the body experiences failure is called strength
(F.McCarthy, 2007).
In general, the mechanical properties of all soils are mainly connected by the shear
resistance of particles between each other. The Mohr-Coulomb failure criterion
states that the strength of dry, particulate material depends on two quantities:
τ = C + σtanϕ
(2.16)
Where τ is shear stress at failure [MPa], σ is normal stress at failure [MPa], C is
cohesion [MPa] and ϕ is soil’s friction angle [◦].
When unfrozen water is present in the soil, normal stress is expressed by effective
stress 2.17, where normal stress is influenced by pore water pressure u. The effective
strength is vital in frozen soils but difficult to evaluate because of ice presence
(Williams and Smith, 1995).
σ′ = σ −u
(2.17)
Cohesion in fine-grained soils exists due to interparticle attractive forces, and in
frozen soils, increases many times because of cementation between ice and soil,
which is much stronger, despite the unfrozen water film between them (Williams and
Smith, 1995). The friction angle component of equation 2.16 is mainly attributed
to the soil phase, with some contribution from fractured ice crystals.
2.1.2.3
Sensitivity
Some soils (especially clays) have considerably higher undisturbed strengths than
disturbed strengths, especially when it comes to frozen soils, due to their suscep-
tibility to any thermal changes within the structure. The ratio of undisturbed
strength (cu) to remolded strength (cur) is called sensitivity (St) (Wood, 1990).
St = cu
cur
(2.18)
Sensitivity and liquidity indexes are highly correlated. There is a trend of increasing
sensitivity with an increasing liquidity index (Wood, 1990) and (Bjerrum, 1954).
In (Bjerrum, 1954), it is denoted that sensitivity is closely related to salinity as
well. When salt is leached out from the soil upon remolding, Atterberg limits
decrease, which causes sensitivity to increase.
Clays are the most susceptible fraction to loss of strength upon remolding. Classi-
fication of sensitivity for clays was introduced, based on strength tests (Skempton
and Northey, 1952).
24
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
25
Table 2.1.4: Classification of clays, based on sensitivity.
Description
Sensitivity
Insensitive clays
0 - 1
Clays of low sensitivity
1 - 2
Clays of medium sensitivity
2 - 4
Sensitive clays
4 - 8
Extra sensitive clays
8 - 16
Quick clays
>16
Most heavily over-consolidated clays are insensitive, as are most boulder clays.
Commonly occurring clays expect a value between 1 and 8 (Skempton and Northey,
1952).
An insensitive clay type might be expected for frozen soil because it is impossible
to remold frozen state samples to the same ice distribution after thawing. Ice in
frozen soil works as cement, capturing the structure of soil particles in a current
matrix, and unless there is a temperature change or applied stress, the matrix is
not easily changeable. Therefore, it can be agreeable to classify the frozen soil
as an insensitive clay category. Checking sensitivity in thawed might give more
information about a potential sensibility if the soil is in an unfrozen state, for any
reason soil loses its frozen state.
Key factors affecting strength loss upon remolding clays are the mineralogy with
particular reference to clay fraction, particle size distribution, thixotropy, and
micro-structural stability of soil (Skempton and Northey, 1952).
Thixotropy is defined as a time-dependent return to the original strength state
following a process of strength loss caused by remolding.
Purely thixotropic
material, after remolding and some time passed, will regain its original strength
and keep this characteristic, no matter how many remolds there would have been
(Burgers and Blair, 1948).
25
==PAGE:48==
26
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
Figure 2.1.16: Graphs, showing shear strength recovery in remolded material
after time passing, for purely thixotropic material (a) and partially thixotropic
material (b)
In practice, clays exhibit partial thixotropic features, which correlate with plastic
behavior. Usually, in natural clays, thixotropic characteristics cannot be accounted
for sensitivity change because they experience little to no strength recovery after
time passing, according to studies (Skempton and Northey, 1952).
2.1.2.4
Elastic and viscous relaxation
Concerning the previous section, frozen soil’s behavior under loading is expected
to be similar to ice.
This expectancy means an elastic-viscoplastic behavior.
This behavior suggests possible instantaneous elastic and time-dependent viscous
relaxation upon unloading.
The experimental research on time-dependent deformation after unloading is almost
entirely performed on constant load tests, where elastic and viscous relaxation
upon the unloading occurs together and cannot be differentiated (Vergote and
Leung, 2015).
In frozen soil compression testing, elastic behavior is influenced by temperature,
where lower temperatures increase elastic behavior because of stiffer soil structure.
In comparison, higher temperatures decrease ice content and stiffness, which leads
26
==PAGE:49==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
27
to higher viscoplastic behavior (Xiangtian and Wang, 2017). Load size and loading
rate can also influence the relaxation of the sample after unloading, as they influence
the failure process.
2.1.2.5
Failure process
The failure process is a mechanism of failure process creation and development
inside the soil upon pressure. The process is time-dependent and influenced by
mechanical and index properties, temperature, hardness, stiffness, size, and loading
rate during compression (F.McCarthy, 2007). This thesis only focuses on and
describes the failure process for compression.
It is triggered by an initial appearance of micro-cracks inside the material that
continue their growth and development, leading to deformation and later failure
(Shunying and Xiaodong, 2020). Frozen soil has three failure processes: initial
cracking, development, and end failure. These types result from either domination
of elastic or viscoplastic behavior (Shunying and Xiaodong, 2020).
The ductile failure process characterizes frozen soil with a viscoplastic dominant
behavior instead of elastic. It is more likely to appear in slow loading rate conditions
because the fracture is stabilized by ductile behavior. The initial elastic behavior
is small, and viscoplastic deformation caused by creep and grain de-cohesion starts
quickly after loading. Figure 2.1.17 shows this process, where after certain elastic
deformation, an initial crack appears in the most weakened location in frozen
soil’s matrix, which might be an ice-filled pore with higher local salinity (stage
I). Elastic deformation continues with the crack developing along the ice pores
and particle surfaces, changing direction and causing it to appear wing-like (stage
II). Further failure process development increases the number of cracks that start
to widen and eventually cause yield, which stops elastic deformation and causes
plastic deformation to start and continue (stage III). The ductile process does not
necessarily cause a fracture in the end. Figure 2.1.18 displays different possible
scenarios for ductile behavior, which is influenced by different ice-soil matrices.
This situation is rarer than the previous one since it requires a columnar ice
layout. The process is similar to the first one, but cracks appear in the vertical
direction, yielding a plastic deformation closer to the tip of the sample (Shunying
and Xiaodong, 2020).
27
==PAGE:50==
28
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
Figure 2.1.17: Ductile process for layered ice-soil matrix. Stage I - initial cracking,
stage II - deformation, stage III - yielding.
Figure 2.1.18: Ductile process for columnar ice-soil matrix. Stage I - initial
cracking, stage II - wing crack development, stage III - yielding.
The second type is called the brittle failure process. It is dominant in elastic
deformation instead of viscoplastic. It is likely to develop under a fast loading
rate where ductile behavior has no time to develop, making it weaker, which
causes brittle behavior to dominate. Figure 2.1.19 shows a brittle process upon
compression causing elastic deformation in the sample that develops an initial
crack (or several of them) close to ends, which continues in a wing-like manner
(stage I). The cracks grow until they reach the sample end and side, creating the
first inclined crack (stage II). Continuing the elastic deformation, other cracks
become inclined, eventually leading to faulting (stage III). During this process, the
sample might not have experienced viscoplastic deformation, which can be visible
in failure after faulting, whether there was any deformation in the sample or only
matrix separation.
Similarly to ductile, the ice matrix influences brittle mode where in the columnar
ice distribution shown in figure 2.1.20, it develops vertical cracks (stage I) that
continue to lengthen under compression until they reach ends (stage II) which
causes first buckling (stage III) which might happen several times before the
sample fails (stage IV). Each buckling produces a single force peak in compression
(Shunying and Xiaodong, 2020).
28
==PAGE:51==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
29
Figure 2.1.19: Brittle process for layered ice-soil matrix. Stage I - wing cracking,
stage II - Inclined crack development, stage III - Faulting.
Figure 2.1.20: Brittle process for columnar ice-soil matrix. Stage I - initial
cracking, stage II - Vertical crack development, stage III - First buckling, stage IV
- second buckling.
The transition between ductile and brittle processes is the last type, where soil
can experience both processes simultaneously or during different stages upon
compression. This transition is usually referred to as the Ductile/Brittle transition
process.
Its behavior appears between the conditions for ductile and brittle
processes for loading rate and domination of elastic and viscoplastic behavior. It is
shown in figures 2.1.21 and 2.1.22 that display intertwined stages of ductile and
brittle processes described before. The layered ice soil matrix develops winged-like
cracks that continue towards ends and cause faulting. The initial behavior is ductile
but continues to be brittle later on. The vertical cracks develop for a columnar
ice matrix and continue to sample ends, yielding and buckling (Shunying and
Xiaodong, 2020).
29
==PAGE:52==
30
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
Figure 2.1.21: Ductile/brittle transition process for layered ice-soil matrix. Stage
I - wing cracking, stage II - wing crack development with primary crack, stage III -
faulting.
Figure 2.1.22: Ductile/brittle transition process for columnar ice-soil matrix.
Stage I - initial cracking, stage II - Vertical crack development with primary crack,
stage III - yielding and buckling.
The failure process corresponds to the behavior type it experienced under com-
pression, which is why it is worth observing during and after compression tests.
2.1.3
Test methods and procedures
Most of the classifications and properties described in this thesis are related to
standard European norms. The methodologies of designating them are standardized
by the International Organization for Standardization (ISO), mainly for geotechnical
soil engineering, which (ISO-14688, 2017) and (ISO-17892, 2017) covers. This
section will describe test methods and procedures for the established parameters
in section 2.1.1.
2.1.3.1
Index properties
Water content is determined using an oven-drying method based on (ISO-17892,
2017). This method involves weighing the sample at a frozen state (usually within
a container of known weight) and then putting it into a preheated oven of 110◦C
for 24 hours. Next, the samples should be removed and weighed again. The process
of drying at 110◦C for 24 hours should be repeated to confirm complete water
evaporation. The difference in mass before and after drying gives water content
value. If the soil’s bulk density and water content are known, dry density can be
calculated with equation 2.6.
30
==PAGE:53==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
31
Figure 2.1.23: Schematic of an oven-drying method for water content determina-
tion.
Salinity is determined by centrifugal extraction using the refractometer method.
Samples are thawed and placed inside plastic tubes. Plastic tubes are placed
inside the centrifuge machine and set to 3000 rounds per minute for 10 minutes.
Centrifuge machine uses centrifugal force to separate soil from pore water and
allow salts to dissolve, creating an upper layer of separated soil and pore water in
a tube. Pore water is extracted with a pipette and put into a refractometer. The
refractometer uses Snell’s law, where water-salt solution bends the light depending
on salinity level (Chaney, 1983).
Figure 2.1.24: Refractometer schematic using the procedure.
A pycnometer determines particle density based on (ISO-17892, 2017). A pycnome-
ter is a flask of known volume with a capillary stopper at the top for accurate
volume measurements. The experiment requires a series of steps to assess the solid
density of soil accurately:
1. Completely drying the pycnometer before using and thawing the frozen soil
sample, and remove any large debris or stones from the sample after thawing.
2. Weighting empty pycnometer (Mp) and completely drying thawed soil in the
oven at 110◦C for 24 hours.
31
==PAGE:54==
32
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
3. Adding soil to the pycnometer and weighing it, which gives the mass of the
pycnometer with soil (Mp + Ms). Next, add distilled water and properly mix it.
Without a capillary stopper, the pycnometer is put into a vacuum, tapping it
gently every few seconds for all bubbles to come out. After that, making sure there
is no air inside, placing the stopper at the top, and making sure that the flask is
completely filled to the lid.
4. Weighting pycnometer with soil and distilled water mixture (Mp + Ms + Mw).
5. Calculating the mass of distilled water from mass difference and dividing it by
the density of distilled water, which is assumed as 0.99802 g/cm3 at 21◦C, which is
an approximate temperature in the laboratory (Fierro and Nyler, 2007).
6. After obtaining the volume of distilled water and the volume of the pycnometer,
the volume of solids is the difference between these two values.
7. The solids’ mass and volume are known so that particle density can be calculated.
The value is compared to a standard literature value suggested for certain fractures
of soils. Known values of particle and bulk densities enable us to estimate porosity
and void ratio from equations 2.2 and 2.1.
Excess ice is determined by using a graduated cylinder, where a frozen soil sample is
put and then allowed to completely thaw (while trying to minimize the vaporization
of water by placing a lid at the top of the cylinder). After thawing, the sediment
and supernatant water volume is recorded using the cylinder’s scale. The method
is not very accurate with fine-grained soils. It can only be achieved when the soil
is fully saturated after thawing or the supernatant water does not show (Williams
and Smith, 1995).
Particle size distribution analysis is based on (ISO-17892, 2017) and uses sieve and
hydrometer analysis to asses the grain sizes. Sieve grain analysis can determine
the particles’ sizes, ranging from 0.075 mm to 100 mm. The categorization of
larger than 100 mm is conducted visually (if there are even present), whereas the
particles smaller than 0.075 mm are determined using the hydrometer method.
Sieve grain analysis uses a set of sieves with different mesh sizes. Each of the sieves
has a squared-shaped opening with a certain size. Sieves distribute soil samples in
2 quantities, where the sieve retains grains with larger diameters than mesh size
and smaller diameter grains pass through. Sieves are stacked on each other with
progressively smaller mesh sizes from top to bottom. Soil particles are distributed
through all of the sieves. A pan on the bottom collects particles that pass through
all sieves. The method uses water and brushes to help pass, especially in smaller
mesh sizes, because cohesion holds particles together (sieves are also placed in a
mechanical shaker for 10 minutes). After passing, sieves and pan are put inside
an oven at 110◦C for 24 hours to get rid of the water. Each sieve is weighed with
soil (the sample is also weighed before testing and accounts for loss). Weights of
soils from each sieve are added and compared to the initial weight of the sample to
account for loss.
32
==PAGE:55==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
33
Table 2.1.5: Sieve mesh sizes, according to ISO 17892-4.2016.
Sieve mesh size
Opening Diameter [mm]
4
4.75
10
2
20
0.85
40
0.425
60
0.25
100
0.15
140
0.105
200
0.075
Hydrometer analysis is used to assess the particle size, smaller than 0.075 mm,
gathered from the pan below the sieve tower after sieve analysis. A hydrometer is
designed to measure the relative density of a liquid, which refers to the ratio of the
actual density of a substance over the density of water. The hydrometer system
consists of a cylindrical stem and a bulb containing a certain amount of mercury or
lead in the bottom, calibrated to float upright in the liquid. Hydrometer analysis
is described in a series of steps:
1. Collecting pan residue from sieve analysis and oven-drying it for 24 hours at
110◦C. Retaining approximately 20 grams of soil for analysis (not the whole sample
from the pan).
2. Weighting and distilling the retained soil with water in a beaker, where a glass
rod is used to stir it. The mixture is left to settle for 12 hours.
3. Flushing any soil clumps by carefully removing clear water with a spray bottle
and replacing it with a fresh one. The process is repeated until particles are
suspended for at least 4 hours. A dispersing agent (like Calgon) is added to help
with flushing.
4. Transferring the solution into a blender and adding 20 ml of dispersing agent if
it was not added in the previous step. Next, filling blender with water to 50 mm
below the rim and then blending for 1 minute.
5. Pouring blended solution into a 1000 ml glass cylinder. Topping cylinder with
water to reach exactly a total volume of 1000 ml. Keeping the solution in a constant
20◦C, for the entire test.
6. Sealing the cylinder with a rubber stopper and shaking it for over 1 minute, by
turning it upside down ten times and gently placing it on the bench while starting
a stopwatch.
7. Inserting the hydrometer gently into the cylinder while reading its values at
specified intervals. Recording temperature each time, to assess viscosity of water.
Storing hydrometer in a nearby water-filled cylinder between readings to avoid
sedimentation.
8. Using Stoke’s law for spheres and calculating terminal velocity for particles that
fall through a liquid by measuring how far and long particles fall, we can calculate
particle diameters.
33
==PAGE:56==
34
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
vterm = ρs −ρw
18ηvisc
D2
particle
(2.19)
vterm is a terminal velocity [cm/s]; ρs and ρw are particle and water densities
[g/cm3]; ηvisc is a viscosity of water, depending on temperature [Ns/cm2]; Dparticle
is a particle diameter [cm].
Cryostructure is assessed by cutting core samples in half along their way and
visibly recognizing the pattern of soil for its designated frozen soil class. Methods
of determining cryostructure more accurately, like remote sensing techniques and
laboratory methods such as computed tomography scanning, are typically used as
well (Gilbert, Kanevskiy, and Murton, 2016).
Freezing point depression can be determined by a temperature logger placed inside
a thawed sample being refrozen. Soil is thawed and wrapped inside the foil to
prevent moisture loss before and during testing. One probe of the temperature
logger is placed outside the wrapped sample, and the other is inserted in the
center of the sample. The temperature is logged every 60 seconds. A sample with
a temperature logger is supposed to be placed in a known, constant, negative
temperature environment. The data is transferred via the TESTO program software
on the computer and plotted as a function of time and temperature change. Based
on the cooling curve, where temperature decreases over time until the freezing
point is reached, the freezing point depression point is deduced by finding the part
where temperature is mostly constant for a significant period (suggesting that the
majority of free water is frozen).
Atterberg limits are assessed using the standardized Casagrande test, based on
(ISO-17892, 2017). The liquid limit is the water content of a pre-moistened sample,
which is placed inside the Casagrande apparatus and joins together for a 13 mm-
long groove under the impact of 25 blows. When the groove closes before 25 blows,
the soil is too wet and needs water reduction. The water content is determined
when the groove closes exactly for a 13 mm distance along the way after 25 blows of
apparatus. Measuring water content with an oven-drying method for that sample
determines the liquid limit.
34
==PAGE:57==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
35
Figure 2.1.25: Schematic of Casagrande apparatus, for determining liquid limit.
The plastic limit is determined by rolling soil into a thread and ensuring the thread
breaks exactly when its diameter is 3 mm. If it breaks thicker, the water content
is lower than the plastic limit, and when the break happens to a diameter lesser
than 3 mm, it means a higher water content. Measuring water content with an
oven-drying method for the sample that breaks with a thread of 3 mm diameter
determines the plastic limit.
Using the liquid and plastic limit results in equation 2.13 gives the plasticity index.
Liquidity index and activity (with known percentage of clay fraction) are calculated
with equations 2.14 and 2.15. It is important to consider an error due to inaccuracy
during weighing and evading portion of water content.
2.1.3.2
Mechanical properties
Mechanical properties of frozen soil are to be determined similarly for ice or concrete.
A series of uniaxial compression tests of constant strain rate for compression strength
determination are performed. According to (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004), the
compressive strength of frozen soil is denoted by constant strain rate tests (CSR)
of 1.0% per minute of uniaxial compression. Ladanyi suggests that peak stress is
not shown for strain as high as 20% for warm, fine-grained frozen soil. This type of
soil shows no peak strength, and it is possible to assume that compressive strength
is the highest recorded stress value during compressive uniaxial constant strain rate
test of 1.0% per minute before reaching 20% or the value at strain equal 20%. The
test is performed several times for different temperatures to assess temperature
dependence on the compressive strength. The CSR test is also made for various
strain rates to show that the stress develops differently with unrelated constant
strain rate values.
Time-dependent deformation under compression is determined by performing a
series of uniaxial compression constant load tests (CL) on cylindrical samples of
frozen soil. This type of test enables us to observe frozen soil’s behavior under
constant stress, which changes over time.
The compression machine gathers
35
==PAGE:58==
36
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
information about time, height difference in sample, and current applied load,
which enables the observation of the strain change based on stress for frozen
samples in time. The tests are performed for different constant loads and sample
temperatures, which is necessary to assess creep parameters for constitutive creep
models.
Shear strength is deducted using the fall cone test. It is a standardized method for
determining the undrained shear strength of soil under the designation of ASTM
D4647, which is an American Society for Testing and Materials. The method uses
an equation to assess shear strength:
τ = Kcone · g · Qcone
p2
depth
(2.20)
τ is a shear strength [MPa]; Qcone is a cone weight [g]; g is a standard acceleration
of gravity, equal 9.81 [m/s2]; pdepth is penetration depth [mm]; Kcone is a fall cone
factor [-].
The fall cone factor has values between 0.5 and 1.33. Its value is estimated to be:
Kcone = 0.37 + 0.1 ln rrot
(2.21)
rrot is the equivalent rotation rate when measuring the shear strength using the
mini shear vane test.
Fall cone apparatus uses metal cones of various masses like 60g, 100g, and 300g,
while their geometry consists of a pointed weight and an angle of 30 degrees. The
cone is released from the support structure to allow free fall into a soil sample
below. The depth of cone penetration also provides a direct measure of the soil’s
consistency and plastic limit. Liquid limit is identified as moisture content at
which the cone penetrates depth up to 20 mm. Samples were tested by fall cone in
frozen, thawed, and remolded states. The information about the undisturbed and
remolded strengths is also used to assess the sensitivity of frozen soil.
36
==PAGE:59==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
37
Figure 2.1.26: Fall cone apparatus, according to the ASTM D4647.
Elastic and viscous relaxation of frozen soil is performed with a simplified method.
The elastic relaxation test uses a compression machine after constant load tests
where the deformed sample is unloaded, and the piston moves away to reach a gap of
exactly 5 millimeters. Next, the machine records displacement as the piston returns
toward the sample. When the machine touches the sample, it records displacement,
which is interpreted as elastic relaxation because there is no break during the
whole test. Viscous relaxation is recorded similarly to elastic relaxation, but the
time dependency is introduced. The same procedure for measuring displacement is
applied, but after some time passing. The magnitude of time is similar to creep
development during the compression test.
2.1.4
Time-dependent modelling
This subsection introduces the general idea of geotechnical modeling, discusses
different types of geotechnical models, and introduces specific models for frozen
soil.
2.1.4.1
General idea and types of soil modeling
Engineers understand soil modeling as an appropriate simplification of reality. A
significant part of soil modeling is recognizing an appropriate level of simplifica-
tion, which means distinguishing significant and obsolete features. Geotechnical
engineering recognizes different models that shape understanding of soil behavior,
structure, history, and complexity (Wood, 2004).
Empirical models have a long history within geotechnics. They rely on experience,
not theory or analysis. These models are closer to semi-empirical rather than purely
empirical because the appliance of known and common knowledge is implemented.
Empirical modeling can provide satisfactory results, although it does not always
follow clear, logical sense (Wood, 2004).
Theoretical models are more universal and elegant solutions to modeling but
usually lack proper usage with no possibility of boundary conditions appliance.
37
==PAGE:60==
38
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
The premise of theoretical models is to assess how soil’s behavior fits into the
model’s framework. Complicated soil behavior, caused by many different variables,
prevents this fitting sometimes (Wood, 2004).
Numerical models rely on finite element methods for most of the work (Zienkiewicz
and Taylor, 2000), (Smith and Griffiths, 1988) (Potts and Zdravković, 1999). The
FEM method implies the replacement of a continuous description of a problem
(which is what theoretical models try to obtain) and finds the solution that is
obtained only at a finite number of points in space and time. The method is only
as good and accurate as the quality of the numerical approximation it utilizes. The
quantities in numerical modeling change rapidly with position or time, so increasing
the discretization density is necessary. When applied to a problem (Wood, 2004),
numerical models should always be verified in reality.
Constitutive models are governed by equations describing the link between strain
and stress changes for any soil element. Each of these models is a simplification
of soil behavior inspired by experimental observations. The point of constitutive
modeling is to identify the vital features of soil behavior in a specific application.
The more complex a constitutive model is, the more material properties must be
defined from many laboratory or in-situ tests. The consequence is that constitutive
modeling can be applied for simplified cases of soil behavior, where only single
uniform elements of soil are active. When choosing a constitutive model for an
engineering problem, one must develop an awareness of soil features and responses
reproduced by the chosen model (Wood, 2004).
Physical models are performed to validate theoretical or empirical hypotheses. They
are associated with the performance of complete geotechnical systems physical
testing. Any geotechnical construction qualifies as a physical model because it
produces a real response. General knowledge of certain material properties, such
as stiffness or strength, provides data that fortifies those physical models’ usage.
The fact that those structures exist proves that the physical models work. Proving
that the model is successful and accurate toward reality like that is impossible
for theoretical models. For them, it is only likely to either falsify or refute them
(Wood, 2004).
The geological model helps in understanding the reason for and nature of features
on the ground. As the ground and soil matrix is not homogeneous, and local
changes in its structure occur, the geological model is essential to propose certain
conditions throughout the discussed area of geotechnical activity. The geological
model does not provide any results or conclusions for geotechnical engineering
but is required to assess its continuity or lack thereof in stratigraphy. The most
popular method of assessing the geological model is to use a series of boreholes that
provide better quality results if their occurrence is denser in an area of interest
(Wood, 2004).
The classification model works similarly to a geological model but on a more local
scale where its assessment provides basic soil index properties for a particular mass
of soil in an area. This model was already described in detail in section 2.1.3.1
38
==PAGE:61==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
39
2.1.4.2
Creep modelling of frozen soil
This section contains a description of six different constitutive models for time-
dependent creep deformation of frozen soil. Some have more than one-dimensional
equations developed, but this thesis only focuses on one-dimensional simplification.
The reason of using one dimensional equations is the nature of experimental tests
which are performed in a uniaxial manner for compression, therefore the one
dimensional equations fit these tests. They also provide the most accurate results
because of the biggest simplification of reality. The stress portrayed in the models
is always interpreted as a positive number. This thesis only focuses on showing
the final equations for one-dimension conditions, necessary parameters, and some
example values for frozen soils, not the equation derivations or detailed descriptions
of obtaining parameters, which are available in the sources provided for the models.
2.1.4.2.1
Berggren’s Model
Anne Lise Berggren developed Berggren’s model, and all its equations and parame-
ters are described and derived in (Berggren, 1983). This model applies to both
permafrost and artificially frozen ground. The model describes creep behavior
in primary and secondary states, which includes temperature dependency and is
verified only for compression.
The model assumes that total strain (ϵ) is the sum of initial instantaneous strain,
primary creep strain (ϵp), and secondary creep strain (ϵs):
ϵ = ϵ0 + ϵp + ϵs
(2.22)
If the time of deformation (t) lasts less than the period of primary creep (tp), then
the secondary creep strain equals 0. We can interpret primary creep period (tp) as:
tp = tpζ · (σθ

(2.23)

with equations:

2.1.4.2.2
Vyalov’s Model
Vyalov developed the model as one of the first attempts to create creep equations
for frozen soil at the request of the United States Army. Frozen soil’s behavior
was determined as elasto-plastic-viscous that undergoes creep, stress relaxation,
and strength reduction. Its equations and parameters are described and derived in
(Vyalov, 1962). The model includes primary and secondary creep distinction, with
temperature dependency, as well as the inclusion of limiting long-term strength,
and is verified in compression and tension. It gives equations in one, two, and
three dimensions. Vyalov proposes that static calculations for frozen soils should
be performed for both deformation and strength in agreement with calculations at
limiting conditions for construction purposes.
One dimensional total strain (ϵ) equation does not separate the primary and
secondary creep phases:
ϵ = ϵ0 + ( σ

[-]; t is time [s]; ϵ0 is an instantaneous strain [-].

Equation 2.32 considers time dependence for strength reduction. If stress surpasses
a certain value, deformation results in a progressive flow stage, after which failure
occurs. The model defines failure as a transition point between secondary and
tertiary creep.

2.1.4.2.3
Herzog’s and Hofer’s Model
Herzog and Hofer developed the model. The authors wanted a formula to be
simple in practical handling, be able to describe performed tests and predict the
behavior under any load, including time to failure. Equation 2.39 describes these
requirements according to the authors. Parameters and equations are defined and
derived in (P.Herzog and Hofer, 1981). The model covers frozen soils’ primary and
secondary creep without temperature dependency.
One dimensional strain after a given time with known stresses is calculated by:
ϵ = σaHH · tbHH + dHH · t
(2.39)
ϵ is strain [-]; σ is stress [MPa]; t is time [s]; aHH is temperature and stress
dependent exponent [-]; bHH is material constant exponent [-]; dHH is temperature
and stress dependent factor [-].
To predict time of failure (tζ) for chosen stress (σ) and failure deformation (ϵζ),
the model suggests plotting (ϵζ −dHH · t) and (σaHH · tbHH) against each other.
The point of intersection is a time of failure.
43
==PAGE:66==
44
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
Figure 2.1.29: Time of failure according to Herzog’s and Hofer’s model.
Model’s parameters from the equation are aHH, bHH, and dHH. Parameter aHH is
temperature and stress-dependent exponent, σaHH reflects the magnitude of initial
deformation. Parameter bHH is considered material constant and describes time
dependency. Parameter dHH is temperature and stress-dependent and indicates
creep deformation.

2.1.4.2.4
Hult’s Model
Jan Hult developed the model. He developed his theory based on certain idealized
materials with well-defined creep properties. Parameter’s equations and details
of his work are described and derived in (Hult, 1966). The model is primarily for
describing creep in high-temperature metals and can also be applied to frozen soils.
It distinguishes primary and secondary creep states but does not include strength
or temperature dependency. It has one and three-dimensional equations.
One-dimensional deformation in the primary creep state is given with an equation:
ϵ = σ
E0
+ ( σ
σqH
)qH/(1+µH) · [(1 + µH) · t

According to Hult’s model, total strain in the primary creep state consists of elastic
and plastic instantaneous strain and creep strain. The plastic part is insignificant
and can be omitted for small stresses, while the elastic part can be omitted for
large stresses. For the primary creep state, where stresses are small:
ϵ = ϵ(i,e) + ϵ(i,p) + ϵ(c) = ϵ(i,e) + ϵ(c)

ϵ(i,e) = σ

(2.44)

in power expression to make equations 2.40 and 2.41, which is derived in (Hult,
1966).
The experimental curve is approximated by a straight line for secondary state-
dominated creep because of a steady state creep rate during that phase. Based
on that, the total strain consists of fictitious instantaneous strain (covering both
elastic and plastic parts) and creep strain.

2.1.4.2.5
Ladanyi’s Model
Model was developed by Branko Ladanyi. It assumes domination of the secondary
creep phase in frozen soil and does not work well when that is not the case. In
most practical cases, however, frozen soils develop deformation mostly during the
secondary rather than the primary phase in the long term. The model includes
temperature dependency and has one, two, and three-dimensional equations. Equa-
tions for strength and deformation are the same. Ladanyi’s idea of the model was
to combine Vyalov’s concept and Hult’s power expressions to solve bearing capacity
problems for permafrost. The equations below are one-dimensional. Parameters
and equations are described and derived in (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
One dimensional equation of strain for a given stress and its duration is:
ϵ = σ
E + ϵkL · ( σ
σkL
)kL + ( ˙ϵcL · t
bL
)bL · ( σ
σcL
)nL
(2.46)
46
==PAGE:69==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
47
ϵ is true strain [-]; σ is stress [MPa], t is time [s], E is time dependent fictitious
Young’s modulus [MPa]; ϵkL is arbitrary small standard strain unit [-]; kL is an
exponent, larger by one [-]; ˙ϵc is a small standard strain rate, introduced to normalize
the equation (recommended by Ladanyi to equal: ˙ϵc = 10−5 min−1 = 1.67 · 10−6s−1;
σkL is temperature dependent deformation modulus [kPa]; σcL is creep proof stress
[kPa]; nL and bL are creep exponents [-].
Ladanyi’s and Hult’s work suggests that instantaneous strain (elastic and plastic)
can be neglected in the most practical purposes of frozen soil foundation design or
when the load duration is longer than 24 hours. However, this thesis will include
instantaneous places as the loading duration for experiments is not long.
ϵ = ϵ0 + ( ˙ϵc · t
bL
)bL · ( σ
σcL
)nL
(2.47)
Upon frozen soil compression, permanent strain at the onset of tertiary creep is
approximately constant for a given temperature and type of test. This means that
constant, permanent strain is used as a basis for the creep failure criterion. In
large time intervals, pseudo instantaneous strain is neglected, and failure stress is
derived from equation 2.47. However, long-term strength (σ∞) should be added
because strength is zeroed as time tends towards infinity, which is only true for
pure ice. Time-dependent long-term strength can be calculated as follows:
σζ = σ∞+ σcL · ( ˙ϵζ
˙ϵc
)1/nL
(2.48)
σζ is uniaxial compressive strength [MPa]; σ∞is a long term strength [MPa]; ˙ϵζ is
failure strain rate [s−1].
Creep proof stress takes temperature dependency into account; based on the
dependence given by (Vyalov, 1962), Ladanyi presented it as a power law:
σcL = σcL0 · (1 + θ
θc
)wL
(2.49)
The arbitrary temperature (θc) is usually 1◦C and value (wL) is an exponent,
obtaining by plotting log(σcLθ) versus log(1 + θ/θc). The closer the value (wL = 1),
the lesser the temperature dependency.
wL =

Model’s parameters are σcL, bL and nL. If temperature dependency is included,

2.1.4.2.6
Modified Nishihara’s Model
Professor T. Nishihara developed the classic model of Nishihara in the 1960s so
that the Chinese government could develop a better understanding of permafrost
behavior. It was during high railroad development and significant modernization.
Classic Nishihara model is a viscoelastic plastic model composed of generalized
Kelvin and viscoplastic bodies. The modified modern version uses this model to
describe frozen soils’ decaying creep and non-attenuating creep processes. The
model is one-dimensional and does not contain temperature dependency. All its
equations and parameters are described and derived in (Shao, Suo, and Xiao, 2023).
The formula for strain in this model for one dimension:
ϵ = σ

Modification made by the modern model, suggested by (Shao, Suo, and Xiao, 2023)
is to correct the viscous coefficient in the viscoelastic element in the model as a
function (rather than a constant) of creep time and stress level. An additional
damage variable is considered to show the tertiary state of creep behavior for a
plastic element of the model.

and viscoplastic parts.
Figure 2.1.33: Modified Nishihara model - from the left, it’s elastic, viscoelastic,
and viscoplastic parts.
The viscoelastic part of frozen soil is mainly composed of nonlinear viscous elements
and springs in parallel, as shown in figure 2.1.33. In the creep process, nonlinear
characteristics are relevant and influenced by load size and duration. The viscosity
coefficient is modified, as a function of creep time and stress level, to describe the
decay creep process of frozen soil:
η1N(t, σ) = η1N0 · (2σ0 −σ

2.2
Climate change consideration
According to a report made by the Norwegian Centre for Climate Services (NCCS)
(Hanssen-Bauer, Forland, and Mayer, 2019), different future climate change scenar-
ios for Svalbard were made, based on gas emissions concluding an increase of annual
temperature, precipitation, frequency of river flow, heavy rainfall, and snow-melt,
as well as decrease of snow precipitation and season. The report developed three
models of climate change based on gas emission concentration. RCP 2.6 model
represents a low emissions pathway peaking in the mid-21st century and then
declines. RCP 4.5 scenario represents a moderate emissions pathway that continues
to rise through the 21st century and stabilizes by the end of it. RCP 8.5 represents
a scenario where a high emission pathway is present and continues to increase
rapidly through the 21st century without any significant mitigation. Tables 2.2.1
and 2.2.2 show annual and seasonal temperature changes for those three scenarios,
half and end of the century. It displays medium (50%), low (5%), and high (95%)
percentiles of absolute changes concerning data gathered between 1971 and 2000
for the Longyearbyen airport area. This area corresponds to a studied location in
this thesis.

Results of temperature change for half of the century show an increase of annual
temperature between 0.7◦C to 4.1◦C for low emissions, depending on the percentile
of absolute changes, 4.0◦C to 7.1◦C for medium emissions and 4.6◦C to 8.3◦C for
high emissions. By 2100 year, the increase in annual temperature is between 0.3◦C
to 5.0◦C for low emission, 4.4◦C to 8.9◦C for medium emissions, and between 7.3◦C
to 14.4◦C for high emissions. Even the most optimistic prognosis results in an
increase in annual air temperature. Considering the current annual temperature of
the airport Longyearbyen area, which is approximately -6.0◦C, it means that air
temperature might change between -5.3◦C to +2.3◦C for half a century, depending
on the emission scenario and into between -5.7◦C to +8.4◦C by the end of the
century. Assuming a middle scenario and medium percentile to be a likely prognosis,
for this thesis, the annual temperature for the study area would be -1.4◦C for half
of a century and +0.3◦C by the end of the century. This assumption would have a
large impact on frozen soil, as air temperature shows a higher temperature than
the thawing point of frozen soil (considering salinity). This would increase active
layer thickness and significantly or completely decrease permafrost loss in that
area. Even the optimistic scenarios would result in potential permafrost loss as the
ground temperature in this location, based on section 4.3 evaluation, that concluded
permafrost temperature to be between -2.0◦C to -4.0◦C under active layer, would
more likely thaw. This prognosis should be considered as a warning towards frozen
soil engineering design methods and either consider complete disregard of frozen
soil’s aspect due to its possible disappearance or engage better external active
artificial freezing methods, introduced in section 2.3. Frozen soil conditions might
be preserved under special artificial conditions and used as an advantage against
time-dependent deformation action.
2.3
Foundations on frozen soil
Several sources (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004), (Williams and Smith, 1995),
(Berggren, 1983) have established that frozen soil, in comparison to standard soil,
shows stronger mechanical properties in general, and the lower the temperature,
the greater that difference is. Using frozen soil in construction engineering is
effective if certain measures are considered. The Arctic, in general, is a place with
unpredictable weather patterns and a harsh environment, which makes construction
harder and more complex to maintain and control logistically. Cold regions have
their own trends and methods of foundation engineering, which have been proven
empirically to work and are less expensive. Methods have already been established
for several decades, which shortens the time needed to train people, and companies
52
==PAGE:75==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
53
already have deals established with materials and equipment distributors, saving
money. A pile foundation is a standard foundation for larger buildings in the Arctic.
Shallow foundations are used as well, but only for lighter structures. Preference for
piles comes from the fact that they are mostly prefabricated and can be installed
mechanically, removing the need to excavate. No need for excavation saves time
and money and does not disturb the thermal regime of the ground, which influences
permafrost conditions (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
This section will introduce and describe shallow and pile foundation systems for
frozen soils, along with necessary settlement equations. An effective frozen soil
foundation fulfills several requirements and constraints to be considered successful
and proper for permafrost foundation engineering.
The first qualification is supporting conditions not affected by seasonal thawing.
Frost-stable coarse granular soils or ice inclusion-free rocks are the least affected.
These materials’ high permeability and uniformity neglect frost-heaving effects
(upward soil swelling due to water and ice increase throughout many season changes).
This mitigates the foundation problem to a minimum while gaining benefits from
the mechanical properties of ice cementation (Linell and Lobacz, 1980).
The second qualification for the effective system in permafrost is supporting
conditions affected by thawing where ice quantity in the soil is significant. This
is very common in fine-grained frozen soils. The low permeability of these soils
means they are oversaturated after thawing, which is responsible for shear strength
increase (Linell and Lobacz, 1980).
The third important qualification is maintaining the already existing negative
thermal regime. This is achieved by using certain methods to prevent possible
warming during construction and the structure’s lifetime. These methods are
categorized as passive or active solutions, depending on energy consumption.
Passive methods maintain the existing underground thermal regime, while active
methods further decrease the underground’s temperature. Passive methods are more
common because they are cheaper and more convenient, for example, thermosiphons,
thermal barriers, insulation, elevated construction, and snow removal systems.
Active methods require more sophisticated and elaborated systems such as artificial
freezing (thermopumps) or artificial ventilation of footings and piles during cold
periods (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004). These methods are used less frequently
due to the costs of building and maintaining them. They also require a lot of
energy since they try to decrease the temperature.
53
==PAGE:76==
54
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
Figure 2.3.1: Passive thermosyphons outside of the clinic in Kaltag, Alaska
Figure 2.3.2: Aurora Research Institute’s two-story building in Inuvik with an
elevated pile foundation.
Figure 2.3.3: Artificial freezing system in construction, showing cooling plate
below the structure for COOP supermarket in Longyearbyen, Svalbard.
2.3.1
Shallow foundations
Typically used systems for lighter structures in cold regions, like small houses,
are shallow foundations. They are typically built as footings of a certain width
(Bsf) and length (Lsf), equal or greater than their depth (Dsf), governed by active
layers thickness. Shallow foundations in cold regions require maintaining thermal
equilibrium, usually obtained by placing them on gravel berm or sandy soil layers
with or without insulation. The bottom part won’t thaw the soil underneath,
causing potential deformation. Excavation and construction are usually done
during the fall season, so disturbed areas can freeze back during the upcoming
54
==PAGE:77==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
55
winter and increase ice cementation. Shallow footings are used only if the pile
foundation is too expensive or unnecessary for certain projects, which is usually
the case with lighter structures that have smaller budgets and do not require high
capacities.
Shallow footings require extra attention to keep the essential temperature after
construction. Elevating the foundation system from the ground surface and clearing
that airspace below the structure regularly (typically between 0.5 and 1.0 m but can
be bigger in wide heated structures) is shown in figure 2.3.2, as well as building floor
and in-ground insulation that dissipates heat losses from the structure. Footing
columns need additional protection from freeze uplift forces created by frost action
so that after a couple of season changes, the foundation might get damaged due to
tension (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
Figure 2.3.4: An illustration of shallow foundation footing in permafrost placed
in back-filled pits excavated below the original ground surface.
Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004
Shallow foundations’ design procedures require assessing these points:
1. Selecting footing depth (Dsf) based on the area’s active layer thickness, thermal
ground regime, and geomorphology.
2. Estimating the warmest temperature below the footing and general thermal
regime along the foundation to determine the soil’s properties.
3. Using bearing capacity theory and choosing footing size and safety factors.
4. Performing settlement prediction analysis, based on appropriate constitutive
relationship for foundation soil.
5. Adjusting and optimizing the foundation’s depth and size so that settlement
and settlement rates fulfill the required conditions.
The capacity in the shallow foundations of frozen soils is based on safety against
general soil failure and an acceptable settlement level. Similar to standard unfrozen
soils criteria, with the addition of temperature dependence in soil’s strength
where the cause of settlement is not due to consolidation but creep (Linell and
Lobacz, 1980). Deformation response due to load is non-linear when considering
consolidation that increases soil’s strength in time. Commonly used Boussinesq
55
==PAGE:78==
56

The above equations are the first approximation with certain assumptions (Linell
and Lobacz, 1980). They assume total stress q = γD and cohesion as temperature
and soil friction angle dependant due to slow consolidation. Typical values for
frozen soil friction angle depend on fraction - for sand ϕ = 29◦−37◦, for silts
ϕ = 15◦−25◦, for clays ϕ = 0◦−10◦.
Consolidation is time-dependent irreversible deformation (similar to creep) caused
by the delayed process of air and unfrozen water migration under a pressure
gradient. In frozen soil, this effect is of secondary importance. The available
literature does not investigate their separation points in frozen soils. The effect
might be neglected in cold, frozen soils with small unfrozen water content. In
"warm" frozen soils, which based on (Vyalov, 1962) and (Andersland and Ladanyi,
2004) consider silty sands at T > −0.3◦C, sandy loams at T > −1.0◦C and clays
at T > −1.5◦C, consolidation should be considered. Frozen soils below these
temperatures are considered "hard frozen" and consolidate very slowly (Andersland
and Ladanyi, 2004).

and safety consideration against failure for dead load plus maximum live loads.

consolidation might become significant enough to increase soil’s strength and
decrease creep rate (Linell and Lobacz, 1980).
2.3.2
Pile foundation
Pile foundations are the most popular foundation systems with heavier structures
or sensitive soils in the Arctic. They usually come prefabricated and are installed
with mechanical methods that reduce the excavation to a minimum, neglecting
the underground exposure, which is why they are valued so much. They work well
on thaw-sensitive, ice-rich, fine-grained soils, especially with ventilated air spaces
between ground temperature and the heated structure (Andersland and Ladanyi,
2004).
The pile’s load-bearing capacity in permafrost comes from the freeze bond between
the soil and the pile’s lateral surface. It also comes from the end bearing of a pile,
but this capacity is a much smaller part of the total capacity and counts where the
pile’s end reaches an ice-free bedrock or dense thaw-stable granular material. Piles
come in different variations based on the material they are made out of. Choosing
the material for piles depends on the temperature profile of frozen soil, soil type,
carried loads, availability, construction equipment, knowledge of people assembling
the foundation, and transportation costs. The most popular types in Norway are
timber piles, steel piles, and concrete piles (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
Due to availability, timber piles are the least expensive for northern countries.
They are mostly made from spruce, fir, or pine. Their heights vary between 6 to 15
meters, and diameters from 150 to 250 mm at the top and 300 to 350 mm at the
bottom. When settled underground, they are well preserved in permafrost but need
special care in the active layer, where they decay and deteriorate. They provide
good insulation properties and relatively high bond strength. Their downside is
their low structural capacity, forcing an excavation during placing that disturbs
permafrost, instead of mechanical drilling (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
Steel piles usually come in pipe or H-profile shapes. Pipe piles are filled with
concrete or sand for a higher load capacity.
They can be driven into warm
permafrost and carry very high loads. Based on experimental results, steel piles
do not experience corrosion in the permafrost layer and only a small amount of
corrosion in the active layer (Linell and Johnston, 1973).
58
==PAGE:81==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
59
Concrete piles are less common in northern Europe and America. They are common
in Siberia and are installed in predrilled and thawed holes. The low tensile strength
of concrete results in cracking under frost heave forces, which exposes reinforced
steel to corrosion, which is why concrete piles are pre-tensioned and precast. Recent
developments of high-alumina cement allow it to cure rapidly at temperatures as
low as -10◦C, which makes concrete piles more acceptable in practice (Linell and
Johnston, 1973).
The pile placement method is another important criterion for pile foundations. It
depends on frozen soil type, temperature, construction equipment, and used pile
material. The most common methods are:
- Steam thawing - Prethawing a hole in permafrost, which is followed by driving
pile inside, (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004)
- Hole predrilling - Rotary drilling a hole before pile placement. Afterward, piles
are driven in an undersized hole or placed in an oversized hole along with soil
backfill, (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004)
- Dry augering - predrilling a hole with a barrel that extracts soil outside. Afterward,
undersized driving or oversized placing piles with soil backfill is made, (Andersland
and Ladanyi, 2004)
Driving - Most common is impact or vibratory driving methods (Andersland and
Ladanyi, 2004).
Pile designing assesses tangential adfreeze strength at the interface of the pile’s
surface and backfill. Adfreeze bond strength corresponds to stress, causing bond
rapture. Adfreeze strength distribution along the pile is based on the applied
load. Strength distribution provides data needed to assess a minimal depth of pile
embedment. Piles that rely on freeze strength as the primary source of strength are
called friction piles, and they are the most common piles in the Arctic (Nixon and
McRoberts, 1976). Adfreeze bond strength relies on backfill properties, temperature
profile, interface type between the piles, load size, and loading rate.
Designing pile foundations must meet two criteria. The discontinuation of the
adfreeze bond along the pile and overbearing settlement is due to time-dependent
creep activity. There must be an acceptable limit of pile settlement within the
structure’s lifetime and an acceptable safety failure factor. Pile design involves
obtaining a pile load settlement rate based on the soil’s creep rate. The method
uses Ladanyi’s assumption that frozen soil’s time-dependent deformation is mostly
governed by secondary creep. The mathematical model takes the creep rate to
propose a solution for the settlement rate considering the pile’s load. In practice,
the ice flow is used because of similar behavior and lack of significant data towards
frozen soil’s creep at stress levels, required for foundation design (Nixon and
McRoberts, 1976).
The power creep equation for piles is based on the state of stress and von Mises
material, where initial elastic strain is negligible, and the secondary creep state is
significantly dominant over other states:

Pile foundations in frozen soils also account for end-bearing strength as a secondary
effect, increasing the pile’s capacity. End-bearing strength in frozen soils uses cavity
expansion theory, assuming the formation of a soil wedge under the pile during
the placement process where its lateral surface depends on uniformly distributed
soil pressure, where the normal component is equal to cavity expansion pressure
(Ladanyi and Johnston, 1974).

1976), which is why it is usually neglected for friction pile foundations.
2.4
Knowledge gaps
The Arctic environment is unique when it comes to natural conditions. There are
limits from hindered logistics, harsh and dangerous conditions, lack of resources, and
small population density. Those conditions result in limited testing and research
capabilities. There has been an extensive increase in Arctic research on frozen
ground in recent years, especially in countries like Canada, Norway, and China.
Scientists try to study the influence of ice and negative temperature on frozen soil
(Statistics-Sentralbyra, 2013). The global research is small because permafrost’s
occurrence is connected to the northern part of the northern hemisphere only,
which covers only a handful of countries that invest in research. However, an
overall permafrost area covers a lot of exposed surface land (Obu and Westermann,
2019).
The complexity of frozen soil’s mechanical behavior increases issues with its accurate
depiction. Standard three-phase soil’s model (particle, water, and gas) is already
64
==PAGE:87==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
65
complicated to understand (Braja, 2008) fully. Adding ice inclusion in permafrost
adds another layer of complexity that increases accurate depiction and demands
more extensive research. This is one of the primary reasons why the knowledge
gaps mentioned below were encountered during the literature review for this topic.
The first knowledge gap concerns the general time-dependent behavior of frozen soil
under load, which is a primary objective of this work. In theory, the time-dependent
deformation of frozen soil is divided into processes of creep and consolidation.
Section 2.3.1 defined consolidation in the process of frozen soil’s compression under
load. The consolidation effect is of secondary importance in frozen soils because it
depends on unfrozen water content, which is considered within warmer permafrost
only. During time-dependent compression, processes of creep and consolidation are
happening at the same time. The general knowledge gap comes from insufficient
resources to separate these two processes during compression. Most literature
studying permafrost does not even acknowledge the consolidation effect during
time-dependent deformation at all (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004), (Williams
and Smith, 1995). Some literature sources mention the possibility of frozen soil
consolidation as a possible influence in the deformation process. Still, they do not
conclude their separation and argument that consolidation is only relevant with
warmer permafrost where unfrozen water content is significantly valued. In colder
permafrost, the effects of consolidation are completely neglected for frozen soil
(Linell and Lobacz, 1980).
An overall relaxation of frozen soil during unloading for elastic (immediate) and
viscous (time-dependent) parts is rarely mentioned in the literature. According
to experimental research on unloading after creep effects, elastic and viscous
relaxation occur together and cannot be differentiated (Vergote and Leung, 2015).
The available literature on frozen soil’s relaxation upon unloading is very limited.
It is implied that an unfrozen clay soil experiences significant time-dependent
relaxation characteristics (Yin and Tong, 2011) and (Mesri and Ullrich, 1978). The
same might be expected for a similar type of soil in a frozen state, where relaxation
might occur, but to a lesser degree, due to increased cohesion from ice cementation.
Frozen soil foundation design demands evaluation of time-dependent deformation
to assess load capacities and settlement predictions. It requires available data
on frozen soil’s creep behavior. Available literature sources suggest a significant
shortage of data for creep behavior under lower stress levels. For design purposes,
many literature sources assume laws and creep data of ice for frozen soil as a
substitute, which has a similar behavior in the long term if frozen soil has a
dominant secondary state creep state during deformation (Nixon and McRoberts,
1976). Most of frozen soil’s theory and models assume the ice-like behavior of
frozen soil (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004), (Vyalov, 1962), (Berggren, 1983).
Apart from climate models obtained by the Norwegian Centre for Climate Services
in the report of (Hanssen-Bauer, Forland, and Mayer, 2019), there is not a lot
of literature that talks about the significance of climate change in mechanical
property loss caused by increased temperature. Available literature implies a time-
dependent deformation for frozen soil but usually assumes constant temperature
ground profiles. Temperature increases caused by climate change in the active
would potentially be an instigator for an increase in the active layer and overall
65
==PAGE:88==
66
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
permafrost loss that might result in a lot of structural damage, where foundation
systems relying on permafrost were used.
2.5
Summary
This chapter contains all the necessary information on models, theory, and equations
to meet the requirements for achieving the thesis objectives. The chapter describes
frozen soil’s classifications, index and mechanical properties, time-dependent de-
formation, and a few examples of constitutive creep models for frozen soil. Apart
from general frozen soil information, the literature review considers an influence
of a possible climate change influence for the tasks set, as well as the theory and
equations behind two exemplary foundation systems used in the Arctic. The chap-
ter is finished by acknowledging possible knowledge gaps that transpired during
the review process made for this thesis.
Frozen soil was identified as a four-phase system of ice, solid particles, unfrozen
water, and air. Its classification is based on a summarized standard of textural
classification, based on silt, clay, and sand percentages (Figure 2.1.4), and cryostruc-
ture identification standard by USCS. Methods used for classification assessment
consist of particle size distribution, identified by sieve and hydrometer analysis
and visual-tactile inspection of frozen samples with adjusted macro photos of the
soil’s matrix.
Index parameters acknowledged for identification are void ratio, porosity, water
content, salinity, bulk density, dry density, particle density, and excess ice. They
are assessed using oven-drying, pycnometer test, refractometer, weighting, and
sizing methods. Salinity is used to assess freezing point depression, which is also
assessed with temperature logging during the refreezing of previously thawed soil.
Activity and Atterberg properties of soil are estimated using the Casagrande test.
The mechanical properties identified in this thesis consist of time-dependent creep
analysis for a series of uniaxial compression constant load tests, compression
strength based on a series of uniaxial compression constant strain rate tests, shear
strength and sensitivity based on fall cone tests, relaxation upon unloading based
on deformation change measurements, and the failure process based on the visual
inspection of compressed samples.
Time-dependent modeling for frozen soil is reviewed and focuses on an introduction
to the modeling theory and equations behind six commonly used constitutive
time-dependent creep models for frozen soils. Models vary with the methods and
assumptions used to accurately depict the process of time-dependent deformation
for frozen soil so that they can simulate genuine behavior in reality. Literature
sources provide exemplary values for models’ parameters to reference expected
values upon the usage of models for the objectives in this thesis.
Climate change considerations introduce the probable annual air temperature
change, significantly impacting frozen soil’s mechanical properties and behavior.
Estimations are based on the official Norwegian Centre for Climate Services and
use three models representing different magnitudes of emission pathways for the
21st century. The results of possible temperature changes for half and the end of
66
==PAGE:89==
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
67
the century in the related study area are depicted.
Frozen soil foundation system assessment introduces commonly used engineering
design procedures for foundations in the Arctic region. The section describes two
commonly used foundation systems for frozen soil. The first is a shallow foundation
based on Boussinesq’s theory for lighter structures. The system considers creep
behavior and bases the equation of creep settlement on cavity expansion theory.
The second foundation system uses piles to determine the load capacities of friction
for the pile shaft and the end-bearing capacity for the pile base. The review states
that end-bearing capacity covers between 1-8% of total capacity, which is usually
neglected in practical friction pile design.
The knowledge gap section recognizes data shortages for the described literature
information in this chapter. Argumentation behind the lack of knowledge comes
from the uniqueness of frozen soil’s behavior, hampered conditions of the Arctic,
and limited to no research funding in countries without permafrost, which is a
majority of the world. Knowledge gaps include a lack of methods to separate creep
and consolidation processes during time-dependent deformation observations, a lack
of research on relaxation upon unloading of frozen soil, and the limited knowledge
on frozen soil creep behavior for low-stress levels, which forces researchers to rely
on the ice data and the assumption that frozen soil behaves exactly like ice during
time-dependent deformation.

==PAGE:90==
68
CHAPTER 2. LITERATURE REVIEW
