==PAGE:91==
Chapter 3
Objectives and methodology
3.1
Objectives
This thesis studies and characterizes permafrost silty loam soils, focusing mainly on
their time-dependent deformation under load and modeling based on that behavior.
Additionally, it checks frozen soil’s temperature dependency for its compression
strength and applies the chosen modeling theory for exemplary foundation systems.
The thesis’s main objective is to investigate UNIS EAST permafrost soil and
determine its index parameters and creep characteristics under uniaxial compression
tests with a similar thermal regime in situ. Collected creep data determines creep
parameters for two out of six frozen soil models described in the literature review.
These were selected based on their relevance and accuracy for the soil type, creep
behavior, and properties. This objective aims to find a suitable creep model that is
accurate enough to simulate genuine time-dependent deformation for Longyearbyen
UNIS EAST frozen soil.
The secondary objective is to observe temperature dependency on creep behavior
based on uniaxial compression experimental testing under a different thermal
regime than in situ. The objective aims to compare the results with the main
objective’s results and observe the potential influence of changed temperature on
compression strength and time-dependent behavior of frozen soil.
The last objective is to pick one creep model from the main objective that most
accurately resembles the time-dependent deformation of frozen soil in reality and
predict creep settlement and settlement rate for two exemplary foundation systems
described in the literature review.
All of the objectives require certain actions to fulfill them, which are:
- Performing soil classification and analysis to determine physical and index prop-
erties.
- Performing uniaxial compression strain rate tests for different thermal conditions
to determine the compression strength of frozen soil and observe temperature
influence.
- Performing uniaxial compression creep tests for different thermal conditions are
performed, and results are used to determine creep behavior and minimum strain
rate. Temperature influence is observed and concluded as a possible mechanical
influence potential outcome based on results.
- Using two chosen creep consecutive models for comparison with experimental
results of uniaxial compression creep tests to determine the models’ creep param-
eters. Based on denoted parameters, the plot modeled time-dependent behavior
69
==PAGE:92==
70
CHAPTER 3. OBJECTIVES AND METHODOLOGY
for conditions similar to experimental ones and concluded the models’ accuracy in
experimental creep results.
- Choosing one of two designed models, based on better accuracy towards exper-
imental results of creep tests, to perform creep settlement and settlement rate
predictions on two exemplary foundation systems for frozen soil based on similar
conditions of foundation and applied load found in the literature that studied
and observed genuine settlements of the same systems to compare their genuine
measures with this thesis’s predictions.
3.2
Methodology
Soil classification and analysis are performed first to achieve the main objective.
It determines soil’s visual and tactile characteristics in situ, particle size distri-
bution, cryostructure, salinity, freezing point depression, Atterberg limits, and
water content. After property determination, frozen soil is classified to choose a
model and determine ground specifications. Next, information about site condi-
tions, such as air and ground temperature, is examined to set and maintain the
same conditions during laboratory testing for in-situ conditions. The next step
involves uniaxial compression constant strain rate tests on frozen soil under the
same in-situ conditions, determining frozen soil strength. Additional information
about sensitivity, failure process, and elastic/viscoelastic relaxation is performed
to understand frozen soil’s mechanical behavior better. The next step used a
series of uniaxial compression constant load tests for time-dependent deformation
examination. Test results are used in creep parameter determination for two
consecutive models chosen from the literature review for matching frozen soil type
and expected time-dependent behavior. The last part of the objective concludes
by using models and their accuracy toward experimental creep test results.
The same uniaxial compression constant strain rate tests and uniaxial compression
constant load tests are performed under different thermal conditions to achieve
the secondary objective. Results are used to determine compression strength and
creep deformation for the main objective comparison. After comparing the results,
necessary conclusions and observations highlight the potential influence of climate
change caused by climate change activities in the Arctic for the next decades.
Achieving the last objective consists of choosing the most accurate model for
time-dependent creep behavior for the studied area and examining frozen soil type.
The model calculates creep settlement and settlement rate predictions for two
exemplary foundation systems. Results of predicted settlements and settlement
rates are made for similar conditions from chosen literature observations, with the
same observations of those systems used in reality. After comparison, conclusions
based on measures from literature and the model’s predictions interpret whether
the applied model shows possible results that agree with real measurements of
time-dependent behavior.
70
==PAGE:93==
Chapter 4
Experimental effort
This chapter describes the steps taken and achieved results from laboratory tests
and fieldwork studies for this thesis. It starts with defining the physical conditions of
the studied location, where soil samples were gathered to reproduce those conditions
in a laboratory environment. Later, the chapter describes the setup, methods,
and test procedures used for the experimental part of the thesis work-line. Next,
the chapter describes the constraints encountered during procedures while using
the techniques and equipment picked. Lastly, the chapter shows achieved results
without processing or interpretations and summarizes them to fulfill objectives.
4.1
Description of study location
The studied area is located on the island called Spitsbergen, which is a part
of Svalbard’s archipelago. Svalbard lies between latitudes 74°N and 81°N and
longitudes 10°E, and 35°E. Spitsbergen island is the biggest island in the archipelago,
with an area of 39000 km2 (Hisdal, 1976). Spitsbergen’s topography consists mostly
of rugged mountains with steep flanks with considerable amounts of flat strata of
sedimentary rocks and mountains of plateau-formed type in central and eastern
parts, divided by broad valleys (Hisdal, 1976). The western and northern coasts
consist of large fjords formed by glaciers some time ago. Two glaciers are so large
that they almost cut the island into two parts (Hisdal, 1976).
Figure 4.1.1: Illustration of Svalbard’s archipelago with islands, settlements, and
surrounding bodies of water.
The exact location of the studied area is inside Longyearbyen City. The city is an
administrative center of the archipelago, located in the central part of Spitsbergen
71
==PAGE:94==
72
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
in a valley on the shores of Adventfjord, surrounded by steep mountains and
glaciers. It is situated at a latitude of 78◦N, around 1315 kilometers from the North
Pole (Winther and Gudmestad, 2023).
Figure 4.1.2: Topographical map of Longyearbyen and surrounding area, from
website https://toposvalbard.npolar.no/
The annual mean temperature of Svalbard’s archipelago is relatively high compared
to other arctic bodies so close to the north pole. Temperatures above freezing
level are a possible occurrence in mid-winter. The cause of such conditions lies in
the ocean’s currents and air circulation by a relatively mild climate, influenced by
the warm Norwegian Current, flowing into the Barents Sea and partially towards
the western coast of Spitsbergen. Large-scale air currents are determined by high-
pressure areas over Greenland and the Arctic Ocean against low-pressure areas over
Iceland, resulting in mild air transportation towards Svalbard from lower latitudes
(Hagen, Liestol, and Roland, 1993).
Annual precipitation on the western coast of Spitsbergen is approximately 400
mm. Precipitation is higher on the glaciers due to the orographic effect (when air
masses flow over higher topography). Svalbard is covered with different types of
glaciers for approximately 60% of total surface area (Hagen, Liestol, and Roland,
1993). The most common type is continuous ice masses, divided into individual
ice streams by mountain ridges. There is a significant amount of large ice caps
in flat areas of eastern Spitsbergen and Piedmont glaciers along the west coast,
where valley systems are filled with overflowed ice, known as transection glaciers
or Spitsbergen type glaciers (Ahlmann, 1933).
In 2024, the average annual temperature on the western coast of Spitsbergen
is equal to around -6.0◦C. The average temperature during the warmest month
of July is between 5.0◦C to 6.0◦C and the coldest period between January and
March is around -15.0◦C (Hagen, Liestol, and Roland, 1993). Temperature in
72
==PAGE:95==
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
73
arctic regions increased on average by 0.3◦C per decade over the last century,
with the largest increases in winter seasons. Climate models for the next 75 years
predict mean temperature increase ranging from 3.0◦C to 6.0◦C and to over 10.0◦C
for Svalbard, depending on the assumed climate model (Hanssen-Bauer, Forland,
and Mayer, 2019). The prognosis of said models suggests that at the end of the
21st century, Svalbard’s climate will be similar to Denmark’s. This change will
drastically affect today’s Svalbard ecosystem (Hanssen-Bauer, Forland, and Mayer,
2019). In the local ecosystem around Longyearbyen, there has been a significant
increase in annual mean temperature and precipitation due to climate change
trends throughout the last years (Winther and Gudmestad, 2023).
Figure 4.1.3: Annual mean temperature change at Svalbard Airport in Longyear-
byen from meteoblue.
Figure 4.1.4: Annual precipitation change at Svalbard Airport in Longyearbyen
from meteoblue.
Higher annual mean temperature results in higher precipitation. In the next
few decades, rain will be a dominating form of precipitation in Svalbard. Higher
temperatures cause glacial melting around Longyearbyen, which increases the water
content and saturation of soil around the city’s area (Winther and Gudmestad,
2023). These conditions accelerate permafrost thaw and the active layer’s thickness,
which worsens mechanical properties (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004).
4.2
Fieldwork effort
Frozen soil samples were gathered in a location known as UNIS East due to its
relative position to UNIS University. Figure 4.2.1 shows the exact excavated
location. The position coordinates are 78.222161 °N and 15.657155 °E.
73
==PAGE:96==
74
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
Figure 4.2.1: Picture from Google Maps, showing the exact position of borehole
excavation.
The excavation lasted five work days, resulting in three boreholes in approximately
the same position. Boreholes were made within 50 cm of each other ( figure 4.2.2).
Figure 4.2.2: Picture, showing positions of three boreholes in UNIS EAST
location.
The excavation process was performed using a bore-rigger Geotech model 505FM,
using the Auger core Drilling method for core collection. This method of drilling is
carried out with a helical screw, driven into the ground with a rotation that cuts
around the soil with a bladed tip. Cuttings were lifted up the borehole inside a
hollow core barrel, capable of gathering cylindrical cores of 45 mm in diameter
and 400 mm in length. At the surface, extension rods are added and connected by
a threaded joint, where the depth limit is restricted to the number of extension
rods available and the weight of the auger drill and rods together. Drill rods and
barrels must be disconnected each time the drill moves back from underground
with the core-filled barrels to avoid damage to extension rods due to wind activity.
With increasing depth penetration, extension rods are higher in the air upon barrel
retrieving, and for safety reasons, rods are removed each time the barrel is collected
from underground. A portable hydraulic piston is used to push the frozen soil core
into a tray, where it is inspected, classified, and packed to retrieve the frozen core
74
==PAGE:97==
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
75
from the barrel.
This excavation method is widely used for all frozen soil types due to its simple
usage, high availability, and lack of drilling liquid requirements. Its disadvantages
are a possible breakdown in coarser materials, depth limitations, and long duration
process of collecting samples (Talalay, 2023).
Figure 4.2.3: Picture from Geotech website, showing bore-rigger Geotech model
505FM.
Figure 4.2.4: Schematic of Auger barrel, used as a tip, for frozen soil core
excavation.
Borehole core collection started after an initial excavation inside 2.0 m depth to
skip the active layer. The active layer contains soil that changes its properties
from frozen to unfrozen, plus the foundation level is always below this layer, which
is why it is not collected. This work focuses on permafrost, which lies underneath
the active layer.
75
==PAGE:98==
76
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
The first borehole successfully gathered frozen soil cores between 2.00 and 3.05
meters. At 3.0 meters, the drill hit a rock that stopped ground penetration, so the
excavation location was changed.
The second borehole successfully gathered frozen soil cores between 2.00 and
6.60 meters. At 6.7 m depth, the drill hit a water pocket, which resulted in soil
oversaturation. The core barrel could not gather more samples, so the excavation
location was changed.
The third borehole successfully gathered frozen soil cores between 2.00 and 4.30
meters. The excavation could have continued, but it was decided to end due to
limited fieldwork time and large cores.
Frozen soil cores were removed from the barrel with a piston and then classified,
inspected, and packed. The packing process consisted of foil wrapping, avoiding
water content loss and placing them inside the plastic tubes, avoiding mechanical
damage upon transportation (figure 4.2.5). Cores were stored in a freezer with a
negative temperature of -10◦C to avoid moisture losses.
Figure 4.2.5: Picture showing frozen samples packed inside foil and plastic tubes,
ready for transportation.
4.3
Ground Temperature
The literature review mentioned the importance of the mechanical behavior of
frozen soil (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004), (Williams and Smith, 1995), (Bray,
2008). It was decided that the thermal regime should be determined where samples
were collected. The point is to find temperature values along depths in time and
decide thermal conditions for laboratory settings for in-situ simulations. In arctic
geotechnics, it is important to perform long-term observations of temperature
behavior on the ground so that temperature dependency is included.
76
==PAGE:99==
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
77
Figure 4.3.1: Picture of the borehole with temperature logger and computer
collecting the data at UNIS EAST.
At the UNIS EAST location (not far from the excavated location), there is a
30.0-meter deep borehole, where the thermistor is placed inside of the plastic tube
(figure 4.3.1). Temperature sensors are placed along the pipe and have an interval
of 6 hours between measures. They measure temperature throughout the whole
year, once every six hours. There are 30 sensors along a 30-meter pipe inside of
the ground. At 0.0 m - 1.5 m, placed every 25 cm, 1.5 m - 5.0 m every 50 cm, 5.0
m - 12.0 m every 1 m, and 12.0 m - 30.0 m every 2 meters. Temperature data was
collected using computer software called FG2-SHELL and analyzed from January
2023 to January 2024 by PYTHON. It was processed to a standard frozen soil
temperature profile, which was described in section 2.1.
Figure 4.3.2: Frozen soil temperature profile for UNIS EAST, from January 2023
till January 2024.
Figure 4.3.2 is similar in shape to figure 2.1.1, which is expected for frozen soil.

between maximum temperature curve and depth, the active layer’s thickness (yAL)

4.4
Lab setup
Laboratory tests were performed inside of UNIS building, using two different
laboratories. The first laboratory is a sealed room with two sets of industrial
freezer doors, separating the lab from the rest of the building. The laboratory
has a set of three ventilators, controlled by computer software and temperature
loggers, maintaining the constant negative temperature inside. Temperature can
be changed with a computer in the opposite room with a small window that allows
for looking inside the laboratory. The laboratory performed experiments and tests
that required maintaining the frozen state of soil samples. The room’s equipment
consists of a stationary saw for cutting samples and a uniaxial compression machine
called KNEKKIS. The KNEKKIS machine is a rig consisting of two horizontal
plates, the lower plate being mobile in the vertical direction and measuring height
difference in millimeters. Upon meeting force between places when compressing
an object, the applied load is measured by a strain gauge in the upper plate in
kilonewtons. Data from uniaxial compression tests of frozen soil cylindrical samples
is gathered and converted into ASCII format using computer software.
Calibration data of KNEKKIS machine during uniaxial compression tests:
Steps/mm - 7015
Load factor (N/V) - 9.81
SelfDefFactor (mm/N) - 0.06
SelfDefExp (mm/Nk) - 0.6
The load cell of a compression machine is a model U2A 10T with a load range
between 50 kg and 10 tonnes (0.49 kN to 9.81 kN). The technical manual for the
load cell U2A 10T is shown in the Appendix.
The second laboratory is a standard room, with an average temperature of 21.0◦C.
The laboratory provides access to essential equipment for tests and protocols in
section 2.1.1 for unfrozen samples. The equipment consists of a Heratherm Thermo
Scientific General Protocol oven with an accuracy of ± 0.5 degrees Celsius for water
content and particle density assessment, pycnometer, scale with an accuracy of ± 1
gram, caliper with accuracy of ± 0.01 mm, centrifuge machine and refractometer for
salinity, set of sieves and hydrometer to assess particle size distribution, Casagrande
apparatus for Atterberg limits, cylinder with a scale in milliliters with accuracy of
± 5 ml for excess ice and other useful tools like trays, spoons, pipettes, cylinders
etc.
4.5
Test procedures
Excavated cores were inspected before the packaging process, which included
touching and lightly scratching them with a knife immediately after excavation
to assess the potential dominant fraction. Touching samples with bare fingers
informed us about the range of frozen soil temperature. When the core melts in
the hands after holding it for a few seconds, it is close to its melting point, but
79
==PAGE:102==
80
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
when it does not leave any sediment on the fingers after touching its surface, the
sample’s temperature is below the melting point.
Borehole cores were transported into the cold laboratory, where they were cut
into samples of approximately 100 mm in length. Smaller pieces were used to
assess index parameters. Each sample was classified based on its borehole origin
and depth and assigned to each test, described in section 2.1.3. All samples were
weighed with a scale and measured for height and diameter with a caliper. Known
mass and volume (based on height and diameter and assumption of the perfectly
cylindrical shape of samples) allow to calculate dry density.
Table 4.5.1 shows information about test procedures on borehole samples, the
methods used to determine certain parameters, and how many samples were tested.
Test steps and details are described in 2.1.3.1, and used equipment is described in
4.4.

The applied sample classification system contains information about three things:
First information is the borehole number (B01, B02, or B03), followed by an
underscore. Next is the depth of the upper part of the sample, followed by a
hyphen, and the last is the depth of the bottom end of the sample. For example,
sample B02_3.90-4.30 means sample from borehole B2 at the depth between 3.9
m to 4.3 m.
Particle size distribution was conducted on two samples separately: B01_2.20-2.35
and B02_2.85-3.10. Samples were subjected to mechanical sieve analysis, and the
residue from the bottom pan was subjected to hydrometer analysis. Both methods
were described in section 2.1.3.1. Tables 4.7.1 and 4.7.2 show sieve analysis for
both samples with information about the sieve filter, dry sample ( sieve weight is
derived already), cumulative weight below sieve and what percent of the sample left
the sieve. Using a hydrometer, the sedimentation method was performed on the
residue from the bottom pan after mechanical sieve analysis. It was performed on
a 20-gram sample, using instruction from section 2.1.3.1. Particle size distribution
for two samples is shown in figures 4.7.1 and 4.7.2, along with retrieved information
80
==PAGE:103==
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
81
about 10,25,30,60,75% passing sizes to assessments of curvature (Cc), uniformity
(Cu) and sorting coefficient (So), based on equations 2.9, 2.10 and 2.11.
Particle density was determined once, using part of the B02_2.30-2.70 sample.
Table 4.7.4 shows denoted values and the result of particle density, based on the
equation 2.7 and methodology described in 2.1.3.1.
All of the samples used for mechanical tests were weighted and measured for height
and diameter in a frozen state. Their volume is assumed to be cylindrical. After
uniaxial compression tests, all samples are cut into portions suitable for performing
water content and salinity checks. Recorded weight, volume, and water content
values are used to calculate bulk and dry density, using equations 2.5 and 2.6.
Salinity is used to calculate the change of freezing temperature from equation 2.12.
Tables A.1 show raw results needed to calculate the index parameters that are
displayed in table A.2.
Atterberg limits were determined using the Casagrande test for four samples. The
theory behind the results is described in section 2.1.1.6, and Casagrande’s method
was captioned in section 2.1.3.1. Since liquid and plastic limits are water contents,
equation 2.3 calculates them. Plasticity index, liquidity index, and activity are
calculated, using equations 2.13, 2.14 and 2.15 accordingly.
The frozen soil group of ground permafrost was determined via visual inspection
of the samples’ interior. This verification, of already tested samples, was used to
identify cryostructure. In total, 24 samples had their cryostructures verified by
cutting tested samples along their length without unfreezing or temperature change
throughout. Cryostructure is identified on an already tested sample, meaning that
their interior design differs from the original form before the tests due to particle
rearrangement caused by deformation. However, identifying cryostructure is still
possible, since it involves finding traces of excess ice in samples and using the
classification described in section 2.1.1.4. Figures 4.7.6, 4.7.7 and 4.7.8 show three
examples of different types of cryostructures identified. Photos were edited and
exposed to high saturation (which intensified the colors), high contrast (which
put emphasis on the color difference between soil and ice), and high exposure
(which increased the photo’s light). This method helped show patches of excess
ice in the samples (joint glaring light white and deep black spots are the excess
ice in the pictures, sometimes necessary to differentiate black spots from excess
ice and dark soil particles. also, excess ice has sharper edges than soil particles).
The appendix shows the rest of the 21 cryostructures of tested samples with
determined frozen class. Lack of equipment and resources for micro-scale imaging
prevents determining an accurate classification (Bray, 2012), shown in figure 2.1.8.
Classification from section 2.1.1, is used to identify cryostructure.
Excess ice was only possible to be determined for a thin 2-3 cm layer of ICE+Soil
cryostructure type sample (edge part of B03_2.9-3.0) due to the used method of
excess ice determination and that the frozen soil was not oversaturated. Excess
ice value is not representable for the rest of the frozen soil, but it was measured
regardless of this small part. Table 4.7.9 shows values for the method from section
2.1.3.1 and the result of excess ice. Equations of 2.8 and 2.3 were used.
Freezing point depression was determined by thawing three different samples and
81
==PAGE:104==
82
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
then using the method described in section 2.1.3.1. Samples were refrozen in
different constant temperatures, to see if there could be any relevance in accuracy.
Figure 4.7.9 shows the cooling curve for those samples. Results are compared with
results of freezing temperature change from salinity and shown in table 4.7.10.
The fall cone test determines the shear strength of frozen, thawed, and remolded
soil samples. The method is described in section 2.1.3.2 and uses equation 2.20 to
assess shear strength. Theory from section 2.1.2.3, as well as its equation 2.18, is
used to determine soil’s sensitivity for a given value of undisturbed (frozen) and
remolded shear strength of frozen soil. The test is performed on three different
frozen samples and repeated three times for each state of soil (frozen, thawed,
remolded). Frozen state samples used a 400-gram cone, thawed state samples used
a 100-gram cone weight and remolded soil samples used a 60-gram cone weight.
Fall cone factor K was estimated as 0.5 for frozen samples and 0.8 for thawed
samples. Table 4.7.11 shows depth penetration, shear strengths, and sensitivity
based on frozen and remolded shear strengths.
Compression strength tests were denoted from uniaxial compression constant strain
rate tests. Each of the tested samples was weighed and measured before testing
occurred. Samples are placed in the KNEKISS machine and secured with a layer
of foil to prevent any temperature change and moisture loss. The laboratory’s
cooling system causes local temperature changes due to wind activity, which the
foil prevents from influencing the tested sample. To ensure the foil’s effectiveness,
a temperature logger is placed next to the machine, where one probe is inside the
foil (and outside of the sample), and the second probe is inside the dummy sample,
which is placed next to the tested sample (in a matter that will not disturb current
testing). Compression constant strain rate tests were divided into three subgroups
according to different temperature conditions or values of constant strain rate.
The first subgroup consists of uniaxial compression constant strain rate tests of
1.0% per minute with different constant temperatures. A strain rate of 1% per
minute is a standard setup to achieve frozen soil compression strength, based on
(Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004). Literature states that compression strength is
either interpreted as a peak stress before achieving 20% of strain or, if the peak
stress is achieved after 20% of strain, the value at 20% strain is a compression
strength. Temperature differences throughout the tests were made to compare
compression strength for different thermal regimes of frozen soil. The subgroup
consists of 2 samples for -2◦C, four samples for -3◦C, two samples for -4◦C, two
samples for -5◦C and two samples for -10◦C. The reason for doing more tests in -3◦C
temperature is that this is the most accurate temperature for in-situ conditions,
and therefore, more research was commenced. Figure 4.7.10 shows the stress versus
strain plot for each temperature example for this subgroup, and table 4.7.13 shows
the samples’ compression strength for comparison. The rest of the tests are shown
in the appendix. The second subgroup is a series of uniaxial compression constant
strain rate tests under the same temperature of -3◦C but with different constant
strain rates set up. This subgroup is not determining compressive strength, because
different values of strain rate 1.0% per minute are not typically used to determine
it for frozen soils (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004). This subgroup observes the
strain rate’s influence on frozen soil’s mechanical behavior. Figure 4.7.11 displays 4
samples with different strain rates (0.1%, 0.5%, 1.0%, and 2.0% per minute). The
82
==PAGE:105==
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
83
third subgroup consists of 2 tests with a constant strain rate of 0.5% per minute
under -3◦C and -5◦C constant sample temperature. This subgroup displays both
observations that were studied for previous subgroups. Figure 4.7.12 displays these
samples’ stress versus strain plot. All plots for uniaxial compression constant strain
rate tests have their values stopped after peak stress commenced. Afterward, the
compression machine decreases the applied load as the sample cannot withstand
more compression, making the data obsolete.
Time-dependent deformation of creep was examined, based on uniaxial compression
constant load tests in different constant temperatures and loads, observing both
influences of load magnitude and temperature dependency on deformation behavior.
In total, 44 samples were tested for creep. 7 samples for -2◦C, 13 samples for -3◦C,
8 samples for -4◦C, 9 samples for -5◦C and 7 samples for -10◦C. The compression
machine provided data on the given load in time and overhead displacement change
in the vertical direction. The load was divided by the sample area to give stress
value and height difference in time and was divided to depict the strain of the
sample. Based on strain in time, creep curves were plotted for all of the samples,
showing results that failed in the tertiary state or after a significant amount of time
within secondary state creep and a small value of strain rate. Figures 4.7.13, 4.7.14,
4.7.15, 4.7.16 and 4.7.17 show plots of strain versus time in logarithmic form, for
3-4 examples for each temperature. The rest of the plots are available to see in
the appendix. Figures 4.7.18, 4.7.19, 4.7.20, 4.7.21 and 4.7.22 show plots of strain
rate versus time in semilogarithmic form for 3-4 examples for each temperature.
The rest of the plots are available to see in the appendix. Table 4.7.14 shows the
observed minimum strain rate and creep stage reached from these figures. The
strain rate was processed using the first derivative coefficient of the Savitzky–Golay
filter of linear polynomial degree and window size of 9. Using this filter resulted in
a smoother signal. Based on constrains (section 4.6), Creep test results skipped
the tertiary state of creep in creep curves, if there were any.
After finishing tests of uniaxial constant strain rate and constant load, information
about the failure process was provided. Most of the samples, after compression,
reached failure, which was observed visually for some samples, and based on section
2.1.2.5, observing a finished failed sample might provide information about the
soil’s behavior. Six samples after compression tests were photographed. Based on
the pictures, their failure type was determined, which provided information about
frozen soil’s behavior.
Elastic and viscoelastic relaxation was performed on eight samples using the method
described in section 2.1.3.2. As a non-standard method, values should not be
considered definite and binding. They were made to show the possible relaxation
behavior of examined frozen soil. Table 4.7.15 shows values of relaxation change
for eight samples, recorded with the KNEKKIS machine. Deformation change was
recorded instantaneously, 3 hours and three days after compression deformation,
which reached secondary state creep for different load values and instant complete
unload. Table 4.7.15 shows raw deformation results for various periods and samples
used to calculate strains and strain rates, shown in table 4.7.16. Figure 4.7.24,
shows results in the form of strain versus time plots. The relaxation rate between
measured values is simplified to a linear change.

==PAGE:106==
84
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
4.6
Constrains
Main and secondary objectives established in chapter 3.1 mostly rely on creep
tests performed in a cold laboratory at UNIS in Longyearbyen Svalbard, which
is limited to the uniaxial compression only. The facility lacks the resources to
perform any triaxial compression or tension tests. It was impossible to perform
the direct shear test, which, according to (ISO-17892, 2017), is a standard test
that determines the mechanical behavior of unfrozen soil. However, frozen soil’s
behavior is more similar to ice (Williams and Smith, 1995), which has different
procedures for testing mechanical properties than unfrozen soil.
The primary testing ground of examined frozen soil, described in section 4.4, is a
cold laboratory that maintains a constant temperature inside. However, the system
relies on cold wind activity from ventilators to keep a constant negative temperature,
which, combined with the room’s large size, has difficulties maintaining an even
temperature throughout. Even small variations in temperature in long-term creep
tests that last from a few hours to days might influence soil’s compression strength
and time-dependent deformation.
To minimize that issue, a wind insulation
structure around the KNEKKIS compression machine was set up to decrease that
issue. To observe the effectiveness of this insulation, a temperature logger was
placed next to the machine with and without wind protection. One logger is placed
inside of the dummy sample, placed next to the tested sample in a way not to
disturb the test (the reason is to accurately determine the temperature in a tested
sample without disturbing it), and the second logger is placed a few centimeters
away from the sample. Figure 4.6.1 shows a picture of the KNEKKIS machine,
wind insulation, and temperature loggers. Examples of temperatures before and
after applying wind protection are seen in figure 4.6.2 and 4.6.3. In both cases,
the room held a constant -5◦C. Computer software called TestoCom SoftBasic was
used to process the data.
Figure 4.6.1:
Picture, showing compression KNEKKIS machine with wind
protection, temperature loggers, and sample placed inside.
84
==PAGE:107==
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
85
Figure 4.6.2: Plot of temperature vs. time of 2 loggers for KNEKKIS compression
machine location before applying wind protection insulation. Logger outside of the
sample (Blue), logger inside dummy sample next to tested sample (Red).
Figure 4.6.3: Plot of temperature vs. time of 2 loggers for KNEKKIS compression
machine location after applying wind protection insulation. Logger outside of the
sample (Blue), logger inside dummy sample next to tested sample (Red).
Figures 4.6.2 and 4.6.3 prove that wind protection effectively stabilized the temper-
ature for the KNEKKIS machine. Before applying wind protection, both loggers
experienced changes between -2.5◦C and -7.5◦C, where the value set was -5◦C.
The error of ± 2.5◦C for frozen soil is unacceptable. Wind protection changed
the outcome, with the logger inside the dummy sample changing temperature be-
tween -4.5◦C and -5.5◦C. The second logger still had big variations, but abnormal
temperature values are only individual through time, suggesting the influence of
spontaneous gusts of wind from ventilators towards the logger. Important reading
is the first (red) logger set to simulate temperature inside the tested sample. With
wind protection applied, the temperature for the compressed sample varies ± 0.5◦C
which is a better acceptable value but still considerably a lot for frozen soils, which
needs to be considered when discussing the results of the tests.
85
==PAGE:108==
86
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
Another constraint of the KNEKKIS compression machine is that it cannot de-
termine the compressed sample’s instantaneous strain for applied constant load.
The machine cannot immediately reach a set constant load value. It was observed
that the loading process depends on the magnitude of the set constant load and
the compression strength of the tested sample. The closer the applied constant
stress is to the compression stress, the longer the machine loads it for a set value.
Depending on the sample and applied conditions, this process took between 5
minutes and 2 hours. For long-term tests, where the loading process consisted
of 1% to 5% of the whole test duration, it was neglected in the time-dependent
part. However, that disregard cannot be applied to the instantaneous strain of
the sample. The elastic part of instantaneous strain was determined with elastic
relaxation, discussed in section 2.1.2.4. Nevertheless, instantaneous plastic strain
is impossible to determine under these circumstances and is therefore omitted. In
practical foundation design, models such as (Vyalov, 1962) or (Andersland and
Ladanyi, 2004) often omit the instantaneous strain for frozen soil altogether as
it is relatively small compared to the time-dependent creep deformation. This is
not the case in "warm" permafrost described in section 2.3.1. This is why, for the
modeling part, instantaneous strain is concluded for each sample from experiments
to assess time-dependent deformation for models accurately.
Additionally, deformation data is gathered with a compression machine that records
applied force in time and uses the difference between the sample’s initial height
and the height change in time to calculate strain. It does not calculate stress, as
no extensometers or strain gauges could record a stress-strain correlation. Young’s
modulus is not determined for the samples, and Hooke’s law cannot be used
to calculate strain. Calculation of strain consists of dividing the difference of
cross-head displacement overtime over the initial height of the sample. Because
of this method, it is impossible to determine the tertiary creep phase’s behavior
accurately. The increased deformation rate during the tertiary creep phase, where
micro-crack propagation, increased water mobility, and structural collapse, makes
an inaccurate assessment of tertiary state strain if it is calculated using the method
of strain calculation. Based on (Vyalov, 1962) or (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004),
the negligence of the tertiary phase in the practical foundation design is allowed
because tertiary state creep is short-term, followed by failure.
Constrain for the KNEKKIS compression machine comes from the load cell’s
capacity (The load cell’s manual is available in the Appendix.), which ranges
between 50 kg to 10 tonnes (that corresponds to a range between 0.49 kN to 9.81
kN of force), while uniaxial compression tests in this thesis, were performed for
loads between 0.15 kN and 3.00 kN. The lower range of loads does not correspond
with the load cell’s capacity. The main cause of this issue is that the KNEKKIS
machine was designed mostly for ice compression, not frozen soil with close thawing
points. Lower values of loads below 0.49 kN were set only for samples within -2◦C
and partially -3◦C thermal conditions. This makes results from these tests very
inaccurate and should be considered wrong if necessary. Other tests of loads above
0.49 kN were made to a value of 3.00 kN, being the maximum for -10◦C, but the
majority of tests were made for load values close to 1.20 kN, which is about 12%
of the load cell’s full capacity. Using only 12% of the load cell’s full capacity might
result in a significant inaccuracy with results, which may result in distortions of
86
==PAGE:109==
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
87
accurate time-dependent deformation identification during compression.
Another constraint was the unavailability to determine unfrozen water content or
excess ice in frozen soil due to a lack of proper equipment. Water content was tested
by an oven-drying method, which only determines total water content without
frozen or unfrozen water difference or temperature dependence. This means that
the results do not split the value of unfrozen and frozen water content in frozen soil,
and ice content was impossible to assess. Without this test, there is no easy way
of determining what percentage of water was frozen at each tested temperature.
Their freeze point depression suggests significant unfrozen water content for -2◦C
tested samples, but it is impossible to tell exactly without this test.
Lastly, assessment of cryostructure and, therefore, type of permafrost based on its
time of formation (epigenetic or syngenetic), is only performed by visual inspection
of macro images. Microimaging is unavailable; therefore, non-visible segregated ice
cannot be explored properly, and ice structure is not revealed. If the non-visible
ice type is dominant, there is no certainty in identifying the soil’s cryostructure.

The curvature (Cc) results are less than 1, suggesting poorly graded soil. The
uniformity (Cu) and sorting coefficient (So) results are large. As a result, soil
displays values characteristic of gap-graded soil, where values are not uniform along
fractions and particles between 0.1 mm and 0.3 mm are missing.
Figure 4.7.3:
Soil textural classification for B01_2.20-2.35 (red lines) and
B02_2.85-3.10 (green lines).
Textural classification in figure 4.7.3, identifies soil as silty loam. Sample B02_2.85-
3.10 is identified between loam and silty loam, but the joined point of textural
percentages is more on a silty loam area (figure 4.7.3).
Based on figures 2.1.5 and 2.1.6, from section 2.1.1.3, curves of particle size
distribution suggest a gap-graded, glacial-alluvial soil. Soil displays characteristics
of transported type soil, meaning that it was created by moving soil particles from
the place of origin to the examined area. Frozen soil from the Svalbard archipelago
in Longyearbyen Valley was most likely formed from an outwash deposited by
meltwater streams flowing away from the city’s glaciers. It is also probable that
this area was previously covered by glaciers during the last age, based on this
analysis (Hagen, Liestol, and Roland, 1993).

Atterberg limit test, results in liquid limit and plasticity index, which are used for
plasticity determination using figure 2.1.13, concluding low plasticity clay (CL) soil.
Liquid limit values classify it as low plasticity because they are below 35%, and
the plasticity index has put them above the "A-line." The soil’s activity was also
inactive and normal, depending on the borehole. The difference in activity between
boreholes is caused by a significantly different percentage of clay fraction between
them. This means the plasticity index is relatively low compared to clay content,
resulting in low activity. Its clay content does not fully express soil’s plasticity and
does not match what is expected, based on clay content alone.

Results for cryostructure show that most frozen soil is well bonded, with no excess
ice type. Samples are sturdy and unsaturated. Nbn cryostructure is dominant
and present in 17, out of 24 tested samples (in 5 of them, partially). Frozen soil
displayed a portion of individual, structureless ice inclusions that were divergent
in density, shape, and size (for reference, the photos of samples are shown in the
appendix). V type is present in 9 out of 24 tested samples (in 3 of them partially).
In the borehole, between 2.40 - 2.60 meters for two samples, irregular, non-parallel,
lenticular-oriented ice formations were present. One sample of borehole B03 in the
3.00-meter depth, for approximately 2-3 centimeters in thickness, showed a layer
of ICE + soil.
Boreholes’ B02 and B03 cryostructures are difficult to assess due to a lack of further
information on the dominant Nbn soil type. Structure-less individual inclusions
may suggest that the presented permafrost is epigenetic, which means that it was
created after the host material was formed, with thousands of years of difference
between. However, visible inclusions are non-dominant, and micro-imaging is
necessary to assess a more accurate subgroup.

Figure 4.7.9: Experimental results for cooling curves in different freezing temper-
atures.
Experimental results of temperature logging do not show a standard cooling curve
from figure 2.1.11. The experiment did not record any supercooling effect caused
by the small sample size. Samples were too few to experience the supercooling
effect, immediately changing their water phase to ice upon reaching freezing. If a
supercooling effect had occurred, a difference in cooling rate (different constant air
temperatures) could have influenced the water’s phase.

The results of the sensitivity test, based on the fall cone test, classify soil as low-

performed to determine shear strength in frozen soil due to the ineffectiveness of
penetration. Usually, values expect a few millimeters of penetration in unfrozen

expected from the fall cone test (the value of thawed soil is more accurate to
expected shear strength values).

Based on curves from figure 4.7.10 and values of compression strength derived,
shown in table 4.7.13, there is a strong temperature dependence on compressive
strength. The biggest difference is between -2◦C and -3◦C, where the soil is close
to its thawing point, and frozen water content is decreased, which lowers cohesion.
High differences between tests with the same temperatures might be caused by
inaccuracies within temperatures across tests within the laboratory, discussed in
section 4.6 or because of different ice-soil matrices between samples. However,
temperature influence on frozen soil’s compression strength is undeniably present,
which relates to the second objective of the thesis.
96
==PAGE:119==
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
97
Figure 4.7.11: Graph, showing uniaxial compression tests for a constant -3◦C
temperature and different strain rates.
Figure 4.7.11 shows that the size of strain rate is a decent influence towards
withstanding stress, failure process, and period. A strain rate of 0.5% per minute
developed a larger strain than other samples. It is caused by the ability of larger
ductile behavior development when stress and creep increase slower (consolidation
might also be present). However, the value under which the sample has failed
is significantly lower than higher strain rates. This tendency is the same for a
lower strain rate of 0.1% per minute, but the sample did not develop a higher
strain than 0.5%. A high strain rate of 2.0% did not develop much strain because
there was not enough time for the ductile process to develop. Therefore, the
sample experienced a brittle process, resulting in a faster failure, which is why the
peak value is lower than the slower strain rate. In conclusion, a lower strain rate
lengthens strain development and decreases potential compression strength if the
sample works under ductile behavior. When the process is more brittle, the sample
might experience a more sudden break, called buckling.
Figure 4.7.12: A graph showing the uniaxial compression tests for 0.5% per
minute and different temperatures.
Figure 4.7.12 shows that even if the strain rate remains the same but is lower
than 1.0% per minute, the higher temperature still results in worse mechanical
properties of the frozen sample. It might lengthen strain development because
warmer frozen soil behaves more ductile, but it is not necessarily a principle.

101
Figure 4.7.22: Strain rate versus time plots for uniaxial compression constant
load tests, for constant -10◦C sample temperature and different loads.
Creep test results show the time-dependent mechanical behavior of frozen soil for
tested samples. Their deformation varies from sample to sample, due to different
applied conditions. According to compression test results, constant load values
were set, as exceeding this value for a given temperature would result in fast failure.
Some samples could not withstand their conditions during testing and failed very
fast without secondary state creep development. This was more frequent in higher
temperatures, especially for -2◦C and -3◦C. It was not always correlated with a
higher load that exceeded the compression or at least the accepted value based on
the compression test. Higher failure and failure at the primary state may result
from losing the frozen soil characteristics. The samples in -2◦C and -3◦C were close
to their thawing point according to the salinity and cooling curve results, which
means that some samples could not withstand much stress. The other reason might
be lower water content or a bigger fracture of soil, which could have decreased
compression strength. Additionally, tests were made with loads below the load
capacity of the compression machine’s cell, mentioned in section 4.6, which might
have resulted in inaccuracies. Tests with lower temperatures of -4◦C, -5◦C, and
-10◦C were more frequent in reaching and developing a secondary creep state and
reaching tertiary state creep after some time. Samples could also withstand higher
load values based on their higher compression strength.
Strain rate-time plots were shown to give information about strain development
over time. If the strain rate-time plot does not show a long-term period of constant
strain rate, it only develops in the primary and later tertiary states. The sample
reaches the secondary state if the plot shows a long period with a constant strain
rate. If the sample, after the secondary creep state, has a large increase in strain
rate value, then the sample reaches a tertiary state creep followed by failure. Plots
confirm that higher temperatures have failed more frequently in the primary state.
At the same time, lower values have developed long periods of secondary state
without failure and, sometimes, reached failure in the tertiary state after long
secondary state creep.

Figure 4.7.23: Pictures of compressed samples.
Figure 4.7.23 displays six samples after compression tests that achieved failure. All
samples have a noticeable, permanent deformation due to compression. Samples
(c) and (f), tested in -3◦C lab temperature, show signs of yielding where sides are
deformed similarly to ductile failure process from figures 2.1.17 and 2.1.18, showing
no significant signs of buckling or cracking. Samples (a) and (b), tested at -5◦C
lab temperature, are deformed on the sides, indicating yielding, with additional
cracking and partial buckling. This indicates the Ductile/Brittle transition process
(Figure 2.1.22). Samples (d) and (e), tested in -10◦C, have a smaller yielding effect
with a slightly visible yield on the tips. Samples’ base part displays inclined cracks,
characteristic of a brittle process (Figure 2.1.19), but since there is small yielding
present, samples display a Ductile/Brittle transition process. Still, there is more
brittle behavior, than samples (a) and (b).
Results of failure analysis indicate that depending on temperature, samples display
either a ductile failure process for high-temperature frozen soil, where viscoplastic
behavior dominates over elastic, or a Ductile/Brittle transition failure process for
medium and low temperatures, where both elastic and viscoplastic behavior is
present.

105
Elastic relaxation displays similar values and ranges between 0.54% and 0.78%
of samples’ initial heights. Elastic relaxation dominates over viscous relaxation
strain, which, after 3 hours, ranges between 0.31% and 0.61% and, after three days,
between 0.13% and 0.34%. The viscous relaxation rate between 0 and 3 hours
is significantly higher than the viscous relaxation rate between 3 and 72 hours,
suggesting the nonlinear behavior of viscous relaxation behavior. After three days,
the Viscous relaxation rate becomes similar throughout the samples. The size of
the compression force during the first step of uniaxial compression seems to have
more influence on viscous rebound than the elastic part. Figure 4.7.24, shows
that applied force’s higher size, might result in higher viscous relaxation. Elastic
relaxation does not seem to follow this trend. Based on the tested method, possible
inaccuracies must be accounted for using inadequate equipment for relaxation
observation.
4.8
Summary
In conclusion, in the experimental results chapter, frozen soil gathered in UNIS
EAST located in Longyearbyen Svalbard was subjected to a series of tests to
determine its index and mechanical parameters. The excavation area was checked
for weather and thermal conditions, and the in-situ environment was assessed.
Observations concluded a mean annual temperature of -5◦C for 2023, with an
average increase of 0.3◦C per decade. Annual precipitation for 2023 was concluded
as 590 mm and increases each year due to climate change. Future prognosis suggests
that at the end of the 21st century, Svalbard’s climate will be similar to Denmark’s
climate.
The chapter concluded the fieldwork by describing an excavation location, the
method and equipment used, and the ground conditions. Excavation consisted
of three boreholes, made using the drilling rig model Geotech 505FM and the
Auger core drilling method. The ground temperature was determined for the
borehole, located near the excavated location, which has a temperature logger
that measures temperature throughout the year, every 6 hours. Data from the
temperature logger was gathered and shown for maximum, minimum, and mean
temperatures, determining the active layer’s thickness as 1.19 meters, depth of zero
annual amplitude between 7.0 and 8.0 meters, as well as mean ground temperature,
which changes upon depth.
Depths between 2.0 to 5.0 meters have a mean
temperature between -2◦C and -3◦C, depths between 5.0 to 12.0 meters have
a mean temperature between -3◦C and -4◦C, and after 12.0 meters, the mean
temperature stayed approximately around -4◦C for the rest of measured depth (for
30.0 meters).
The chapter assessed used laboratory equipment and tools as well as methods,
determining necessary properties that were already discussed and described in the
literature review section (2.1.3.1 and 2.1.3.2). It also concluded the constraints
of the methods and equipment, showing the encountered limits. Most constraints
encountered in the testing process were due to the KNEKKIS compression machine
and cold laboratory conditions. The equipment used, along with test procedures,
needed to be monitored and observed to decrease inaccuracy. The compression
105
==PAGE:128==
106
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
machine’s limited capabilities made determining tertiary state creep for time-
dependent deformation impossible. No tensometers made the observation limited.
The machine required windproof insulation due to the lab’s temperature inaccura-
cies. Additionally, the load cell’s capacity range differs from the range of used loads.
It makes the majority of test results for samples of -2◦C and part of results for
samples of -3◦C to be inaccurate, and other tests, using around 12% of load cell’s
full capacity, are subject to significant inaccuracies too. Due to limited equipment,
other constraints occurred, resulting in difficulties in cryostructure identification or
unfrozen water content determination in frozen samples.
Experimental results of particle size distribution classified examined soil as gap-
graded, glacial-alluvial, silty loam soil for ISO classification. The salinity test
concluded the salinity range was between 1.0% to 3.5% and observed its peak,
between 5.0 to 5.5-meter depth corresponding to the local sea level. Water content
ranged approximately between 20.0 to 30.0% without any trends. Atterberg limit
determined examined soil as low plasticity clay with inactive to normal activity,
meaning that clay fraction plays a normal to the low role for plasticity.
Frozen soil evaluation consisted of checking the soil’s cryostructure, determining it
to be mostly well-bonded, unsaturated frozen soil with no visible excess ice (Nbn)
along boreholes. Frozen soil has some visible individual, structureless ice inclusions
on certain depths (Vx, SI) and a small layer of ICE+soil class in a 3.0-meter
depth. Excess ice for this part could be determined with the value of 69.85%.
Additionally, the freezing point depression test was determined three times, using
the temperature measuring method upon refreezing thawed soil, to observe cooling
curves, concluding no supercooling effect and freezing point depression values that
were accurate with salinity results.
Mechanical properties were examined for samples, starting with the fall cone
test, determining the shear strength of frozen, thawed, and remolded samples and
sensitivity. Conclusions deemed soil low-sensitive, meaning that differences in
shear strength before and after remolding occur. Frozen soil’s shear strength is
not typically determined with a fall cone test, and results need to consider this,
as values for thawed soil shear strength are a hundred times lower, suggesting
inaccuracy for frozen soil due to the lack of penetration possibility by a fall cone.
Compression test results have been grouped by samples’ temperatures and different
constant strain rates. Results suggest that temperature and strain rate greatly
influence compressive strength’s size. High-temperature frozen soil has a few times
lesser compression strength than low-temperature frozen soil, and small temperature
changes are significant. The same goes for strain rate, which influences ductile and
brittle development. Ductile development progresses at low strain rates, influencing
the failure process and strain. Results for low strain rates show lower compression
strength values and longer strain development. A high strain rate resulted in higher
brittle behavior, whereas sudden failure with small ductile development resulted
in low strain development and lesser compression strength. A medium strain
rate of 1.0% per minute shows the biggest compression strength, where ductile
development was just enough to achieve it, but strain development is smaller than
compressions of lower constant strain rates.
106
==PAGE:129==
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
107
Creep test results were grouped, based on temperature conditions.
Different
constant loads were tested for soils, which resulted in a series of different time-
dependent deformations. Results show a higher tendency to achieve mostly only
primary state creep in high-temperature frozen soils. Lower temperature increases
the chances that the sample reaches secondary or tertiary state creep, depending on
the applied load. Lower-temperature frozen soils could withstand larger loads due
to their significant increase in compression strength. Results gathered information
about minimum strain rates and the reached creep state.
After compression and creep tests, the failure process of some samples was de-
termined by studying already compressed samples. Depending on the strain rate
and temperature of the tested samples, samples displayed mostly ductile and
ductile/brittle transition processes. Results suggest a dominant part of viscoplastic
behavior instead of elastic behavior.
Results of elastic and viscous relaxation observations concluded a very small but
existent rebound relative to compression. Elastic strain ranged between 0.54% to
0.78%, supporting results of the failure process that established a small significance
of elastic behavior. Viscous relaxation has a similar magnitude as elastic, with a
very low rate. Relaxation was measured after 3 hours and later after three days
from the end of compression and complete unloading, showing strains of around
0.5% after 3 hours and 0.3% after three days, and decreasing strain rate means that
viscous rebound might not even exceed 2% of initial height after compression.
107
==PAGE:130==
108
CHAPTER 4. EXPERIMENTAL EFFORT
108
==PAGE:131==
Chapter 5
Time-dependent model
characterization
This chapter aims to choose two fitting consecutive models from section 2.1.4.2
in the literature review and use experimental creep results of stress-strain in time
relation to determine models’ parameters and calculate strain from them based on
constant stress and temperature. Both models and experimental creep curves are
compared to see the models’ accuracy and efficiency in time-dependent deformation
of frozen soil.
5.1
Choice of two models
In a literature review part, six creep models were introduced. Berggren’s model is
universal for frozen soil but requires an unfrozen water content test for parameters
assessment (Berggren, 1983). An unfrozen water content test was not performed
for this thesis, so Berggren’s model cannot be used. Herzog’s and Hofer’s model is
simple and tends to lack accuracy in creep curve assessment. This model is useful
when creep tests are evaluated without strength or temperature consideration. The
model has an accurate approach towards the time of failure (P.Herzog and Hofer,
1981), but other than that, it is not very useful since temperature dependency is
being considered in this thesis. Hult’s model was primarily developed for high-
temperature metals and applied to frozen soils, showing good potential for usage.
However, it does not include strength or temperature dependencies, like Herzog’s
and Hofer’s models. It is also a model with many parameters to assess, reflecting
on accuracy (Hult, 1966). Modified Nishihara’s model is an advanced approach to a
well-developed model for ice-rich soils that considers both strength and temperature
dependency. Modern modification enhances its potential, introducing a decay creep
process and damage variable that attempts to assess tertiary creep state accurately
(Shao, Suo, and Xiao, 2023). However, due to established constraints (section
4.6), tertiary state creep is not included in time-dependent deformation evaluation.
The model is unnecessary and impractical without needing tertiary state creep
determination.
Ladanyi’s model is often used and was developed for ice-rich soils with long-
term secondary state creep deformation with strain rate estimated as constant
(Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004). The assumption of dominant secondary state
creep over other phases is a usual expected behavior of frozen soil. Ladanyi’s
model is a good fit for frozen soil behavior, as observed in this study. Many tests
experienced long-term secondary state creep, similar to pure ice behavior under
compression.
Vyalov’s model was developed primarily for ice-poor soils, based on changing
109
==PAGE:132==
110
CHAPTER 5. TIME-DEPENDENT MODEL CHARACTERIZATION
strain rates during the primary state, which is highly developed for ice-poor soils
(Vyalov, 1962). The model distinguishes primary and secondary states, temperature
dependency, and limiting long-term strength. Vyalov’s model has a good chance of
accurately representing creep results for examined frozen soil.
The chosen models of Ladanyi and Vyalov will produce different results for frozen
soil’s creep behavior. Their differences in assumptions and usability of either
ice-rich or ice-poor soils might provide compelling results for examined frozen soil.
5.2
Identification of Ladanyi’s model parameters
Ladanyi’s model uses equation 2.47 for strain calculation based on passing the time
and stress value at a given time. Model’s parameters are σcL, bL, nL but considering
temperature dependency, parameter σcL is divided into two different parameters
σcL0 and wL, used in equation 2.49. Based on the experimental results of particular
tests, instantaneous strain is included to show the accuracy of secondary state
evaluation.
Assessment of Ladanyi’s parameters does not include all of the tested samples.
Tests go through selection, where experiments that did not reach secondary state
creep, or their secondary state creep was not dominant, are excluded. The minimum
strain rate is denoted as a mean value of strain rate during the secondary state.
This method is because the theory behind the secondary state only assumes a
constant strain rate. In reality, the strain rate during the secondary state slowly
decreases until it reaches zero, or the tertiary state phase starts and accelerates the
strain rate until the soil fails. The strain rate is considered constant because change
is very small compared to primary or tertiary states and changes in time. Table
5.2.1 shows chosen samples for Ladanyi’s parameters assessment, their constant
stresses, and derived minimum strain rate values.
110
==PAGE:133==
CHAPTER 5. TIME-DEPENDENT MODEL CHARACTERIZATION

5.3
Identification of Vyalov’s model parameters
Vyalov’s model uses equation 2.31 for strain calculation based on stress at a given
time. Model’s parameters are mV , λV and RV , but temperature dependency divides
parameter RV , into ωV and kV . Assessment of Vyalov’s parameters is based on
strain at different times. If two tests of the same temperature and different constant
stress advance into the secondary state at completely different times, confusion in
the numbers of this model is produced.
Similarly to Ladanyi’s model, certain tests were chosen for parameter determination.
Tests with similar periods of secondary state beginning are ideal. Table 5.3.1 shows
used samples and two selected times with corresponding strains. Plotting stress
σ, against selected strains ϵ from selected times t1 and t2 in log-log diagram
for different temperatures and drawing their trendline, which intersection with
strain ϵ = 1 is denoted as values of parameter AV . Inclination of trendlines gives
parameter mV . mV values are averaged within the same temperatures by selected
times. Ideally, parameter mV should be parallel with times and temperatures, but
in reality, values vary. Figure 5.3.1, shows plots and denotes values of AV and mV
for temperatures -3◦C and -10◦C. Figure A.43 show temperatures -2◦C, -4◦C and
-5◦C for parameters.

5.4
Model comparison for experimental results
Ladanyi’s and Vyalov’s models are used using stress data gathered from experi-
mental results. Experimental and model strains are displayed on a series of graphs
to show resemblances and differences. Results are displayed in plots of strain [-]
versus time [s]. Results were compared to samples that were used for both models.
Model results include a certain instantaneous strain value. Adjusting models to
accurate assumptions that include instantaneous strain was necessary to compare
them to experimental results.

Vyalov’s and Ladanyi’s model results are comparable to experimental results for
118
==PAGE:141==
CHAPTER 5. TIME-DEPENDENT MODEL CHARACTERIZATION
119
frozen soil’s time-dependent deformation analysis. Ladanyi’s model is designed
for long-term secondary state creep, being the most accurate for results, with
relatively short primary states and secondary states that last for most of the
time-dependent deformation, like in figures 5.4.3 and 5.4.4. The downside of using
this model is that instantaneous strain was derived from experimental results for
each sample. In practice, instantaneous strain is either assumed or additionally
calculated. The point is that this strain cannot be calculated using equation 2.47.
In Ladanyi’s model, instantaneous strain is not instantaneous because the model
does not include the primary state, meaning that instantaneous strain includes
strain developed under the primary state and the strain upon loading (elastic and
plastic deformation without time consideration). Ladanyi’s model loses accuracy if
the primary creep state is dominant or a dominant state of overall time-dependent
deformation. Semilogarythmic plots show that Ladanyi has a decent expectancy
for constant strain rate in further creep deformation.
Vyalov’s model expects a much lesser instantaneous strain than Ladanyi’s (if there
is any) because it includes both primary and secondary states. The accuracy of
experimental results for this model differs. Some samples show overall accurate
results in experiments for both primary and secondary states of creep. Other
samples have less accurate experimental results. A possible cause of this might lie
in its temperature dependence. Upon examining Vyalov’s model, it is clear that
even small changes in temperature during testing cause a significant change in
strain behavior. The model is also primarily designed for ice-poor soil, explaining
its inaccuracy for examined ice-rich frozen soil. Vyalov’s results display higher
accuracy for warmer temperatures, shown in figures 5.4.1 and 5.4.2, than Ladanyi’s
model. These examples developed their secondary state creep later than lower
temperatures, meaning the primary state lasted longer. This is where Vyalov’s
model showed higher accuracy towards experimental values than Ladanyi’s. For a
longer time duration, Vyalov misses on deformation, in comparison to Ladanyi.
5.5
Summary
The time-dependent model characterization chapter compared two chosen consti-
tutive creep models with experimental results of uniaxial compression constant
load tests that studied the time-dependent deformation of UNIS EAST’s frozen
soil. Out of six different models, Vyalov’s and Ladanyi’s models were chosen based
on their significant and common usability in arctic engineering and the fact that
they fit the described soil’s conditions and behavior well. Both models assume and
focus on different aspects of time-dependent deformation for frozen soil, which is
additionally informative for accuracy in different time-dependent behaviors.
Ladanyi’s model is usually used for ice-rich soils that develop long-term secondary
state creep, similar to ice. It assumes an overall domination of secondary state
creep, which is most often the case in practice for ice-rich frozen soil’s behavior
under constant load. It uses Hult’s power expressions and Vyalov’s concepts to
solve bearing capacity problems. The stress-strain relation is shown in equation
2.46. The model uses a straight-line approximation instead of creep curves to
evaluate creep deformation. The model was chosen due to its accurate assumption
119
==PAGE:142==
120
CHAPTER 5. TIME-DEPENDENT MODEL CHARACTERIZATION
of dominant secondary state creep, which is confirmed by a lot of different literature
sources (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004), (Berggren, 1983), (Williams and Smith,
1995). The model is simple in parameter assessment and includes additional
temperature and strength dependency.
Vyalov’s model is commonly used for ice-poor soils undergoing elasto-plastic-viscous
material behavior with time-dependent creep deformation, stress relaxation, and
strength reduction under compression. It includes time dependency and limiting
long-term strength. It includes primary and secondary state creep distinction. The
stress-strain relation is shown in equation 2.31. In most cases, Vyalov’s model
neglects instantaneous deformation without significant error, sometimes including
a possible instantaneous strain. The model is based on a changing strain rate in
the primary state, highly developed in ice-poor or warmer frozen soils. It was
chosen based on its overall effective assessment and simplicity of usage. The model
also contrasts Ladanyi’s model, which is only assessed for the secondary state and
neglects the primary state. Common usage of Ladanyi’s and Vyalov’s models for
ice-rich colder and ice-poor warmer frozen soils adds to the comparison of both
models.
Ladanyi’s parameters are denoted, using values of constant stress and assessed
constant minimum creep rates from secondary state creep data for each sample
in log-log diagrams for attributed temperatures. Drawn straight trendline for
each temperature and an intercept with a small standard strain rate of ( ˙ϵc =
1.67·10−7 s−1). Intercept gives values of creep proof stress σcL, for each temperature.
The inverted slope of trendlines is parameter nL. Semi-logarithmic diagram of
denoted σcL values, against absolute temperatures, creates trendline where intercept
with temperature 0◦C, gives temperature dependant parameter σcL0. Plotting σcL
against (1 + θ
θc) in log-log program and drawing straight trendline, gives parameter
wL as trendline’s slope. The last parameter is calculated using equation 5.1 and
denoted values of other parameters with each data sample tested. Table 5.2.2
shows overall parameters’ results.
Results of parameters show similar values expected of ice-rich soil, shown in table
2.1.10. Parameter nL has the biggest differences among temperatures, with the
biggest difference for temperature -10◦C. Oddly, this value is the furthest out of the
rest and has the most expected value for frozen soil, which in literature is usually
considered to be close to n = 3 (Andersland and Ladanyi, 2004), (Berggren, 1983).
Vyalov’s parameters are denoted, using graphical methods too. It uses two selected
times, according to strain, with assigned constant load conditions.
Series of
trendlines from log-log diagram of denoted strains, for selected times in each stress
for each temperature and time, give parameter mV as inclination. The intercept
of trendlines and strain ϵ = 1 gives parameter AV , which plotted against selected
times for each temperature in the log-log diagram, gives parameter λV as the
inclination of straight trendlines of results and intercept of t = 3600 s, gives a
parameter RV . Lastly, log-log diagram of parameter RV and (1 + θ
θc), gives a
parameter kV as inclination of trendline, while inclination of θ = 1◦C give the last
parameter of ωV .
Results of Vyalov’s parameter are comparable to table 2.1.7 for Stjørdal clay, mostly
120
==PAGE:143==
CHAPTER 5. TIME-DEPENDENT MODEL CHARACTERIZATION
121
showing expected results for ice-rich soil. Differences between parameters mV and
λV are significant to a degree, but the averaged value is denoted for a similar
parametrization, shown in other literature (Berggren, 1983), (Vyalov, 1962).
Parameters of models, use equations of strains for them, creating similar creep
curves for a series of tested samples. It was necessary to add an expected instanta-
neous strain for each sample for Ladanyi’s model and sometimes a much smaller
instantaneous strain for Vyalov’s model to compare experimental creep curves and
model creep curves. Results of creep curve data are shown in a series of sixteen
tests in figures 5.4.1, 5.4.2, 5.4.3 and 5.4.4.
The resulting model creep curves are similar to the experimental curves for tested
samples. Ladanyi’s model lacks accuracy in the primary state because it completely
neglects it, but if samples have highly developed and dominant secondary state,
which is common for lower temperatures, seen in figures 5.4.3 and 5.4.4, showing
accurate results to experimental data. Ladanyi’s model is more accurate and has
longer secondary state creep. The only downside is that comparison is needed to
predict instantaneous strain, denoted by experimental results. Without experi-
mental results, instantaneous strain should be included using other experimental
or mathematical methods or be assumed as a safety factor value for expectations
under certain stress values. Vyalov’s model mostly neglects this instantaneous
strain. Still, some examples must include a small portion of instantaneous strain
before starting the primary state of creep to be an accurate representation. Vyalov’s
model shows better accuracy towards experimental results in warmer temperatures,
shown in figures 5.4.1 and 5.4.2 since they had longer developments of primary
states. Vyalov’s model works better in ice-poor soils, where the primary state has
time to develop. However, calculations showed high-temperature dependence for
the model, where changing even a fraction of temperature (for example, 0.1◦C)
influences strain values significantly. Overall, Vyalov’s model has a better accuracy
of time-dependent deformation, including the primary state. However, based on
literature and observations, frozen soil typically has a short-term primary state
creep and long-term secondary state creep. It is better to assume instantaneous
strain for both elastic, plastic, and primary state deformation as Ladanyi’s model
does. In practice, assessing accurate expected time dependence long-term is easier
because the creep effect is a long-term phenomenon.

CHAPTER 5. TIME-DEPENDENT MODEL CHARACTERIZATION
