==PAGE:145==
Chapter 6
Application to foundation design
This chapter uses equations and knowledge from section 2.3.1 and results of model
parameters for possible determinations of settlements and bearing capacities of
two typical foundation engineering systems used in the Arctic. Based on the
results and expectations of the examined models, Ladanyi’s model was chosen as
a leading factor for foundation determination. This is because Ladanyi’s model
has the most practical approach to the problem of time-dependent deformation,
assuming dominant long-term secondary state creep with a neglected primary state.
Foundation predictions cover periods of years for time-dependent deformation. If
Vyalov’s model had been used, the accuracy with initial deformation would have
been better than Ladanyi’s. Still, in terms of long-term predictions for decades,
Ladanyi’s model fits better for assessment.
6.1
Prediction of time-dependent settlements
With the models chosen for calculations, foundation systems are chosen, and the
determined parameters for similar thermal regimes explained in section 4.3 will be
given. The chapter uses typical values of foundation geometries and loads applied
to them based on literature analysis from different sources about real cases in the
Arctic, where structures were built and calculated. That way, predicting future
settlements for certain structures with already built-in conditions applied in this
thesis is possible.
6.1.1
Shallow foundation
Established equation 2.72 from the literature on shallow foundations, based on
cavity expansion theory and valid for homogeneous frozen soil conditions below the
footing, is used to predict settlements of time-dependent deformation for applied
geometrical and environment conditions of shallow foundation and certain total
load applied.
In comparison, the case of Vyalov’s experimental results on the Long-term set-
tlement of foundation on Permafrost (Vyalov, 1978), made an experiment on
long-term loading plate, imitating shallow foundation of circular plate with di-
ameter of D = 0.705 m, under constant load of q = 0.245 MPa, embedded in
plastic-frozen silty clay, 3 meters deep at temperatures between -0.1◦C to -1.5◦C.
The settlement was monitored for 20 years. Diameter, geometry, and total load of
footing, from (Vyalov, 1978), is used for Ladanyi’s parameters, displayed in table
5.2.2 and assessed conditions of ground temperatures from section 4.3.
A shallow foundation is placed below the active layer, which was determined to be
about 1.2 meters thick below the surface. It is safe to assume the foundation is
123
==PAGE:146==
124
CHAPTER 6. APPLICATION TO FOUNDATION DESIGN
placed at around 2.0 meters deep below the surface. A shallow foundation will not
exceed 5 meters in depth, meaning that based on figure 4.3.1, it is expected that
soil will work under between -2◦C and -3◦C of permafrost conditions. Ladanyi’s
parameters of σcLθ, bL and nL for -2◦C and -3◦C temperature was taken for range
of settlement evaluation. The value of the strain rate is determined based on
experimental results of a constant load test that had a similar total load as one
from Vyalov’s paper. For -2◦C, strain rate equals ˙ϵc = 1.46 · 10−5 s−1 and for -3◦C,
strain rate equals ˙ϵc = 1.32 · 10−7 s−1. Results from (Vyalov, 1978) and Ladanyi’s
parameters are using equation 2.72 to display settlement from creep in time 6.1.1.
Results do not account for instantaneous strain.
Figure 6.1.1: The plots of experimental settlement from (Vyalov, 1978) and
calculated future settlement for Ladanyi’s model under the same conditions of
circular D = 0.705 m footing under q = 0.245 MPa load.
Results of (Vyalov, 1978) and calculated settlements from Ladanyi’s model are
unusually shown in plot of settlement in centimeters and time in years because
they are more convenient and clear in showing values. Vyalov’s results show a
nonlinear shape of the settlement curve, which can be expected with combined
effects of distortional creep and consolidation. The creep effect continues settlement
linearly, but the impact of consolidation that squeezes out the water from the
soil increases its strength, and in response, soil hardens, and the effects of creep
deformation decrease, causing a nonlinear response with decreasing deformation
in time. Consolidation is significant, as frozen soil works between -0.1◦C and
-1.5◦C which, probably due to salinity, is very close to the thawing point of the
sample. Therefore, unfrozen water causes the consolidation effect. Ladanyi’s model
results are based on an equation without consolidation during time-dependent
deformation; therefore, the results are linear. Ground temperature varies between
-2◦C to -3◦C, depending on the season. The freezing point was denoted based
on salinity, giving a range of -0.7◦C to -1.7◦C. Temperature underground for the
foundation is lower, but not significantly; therefore, some local unfrozen water
patches might be present. This increased strength through consolidation effects
124
==PAGE:147==
CHAPTER 6. APPLICATION TO FOUNDATION DESIGN
125
would not be significant on low-temperature permafrost. Observations show that
considering constant temperature through the next 20 years, frozen soil in Svalbard,
under the same conditions and footing as from the (Vyalov, 1978), experiencing
non-linear settlement in a range of temperatures -2◦C to -3◦C, which is displayed
in figure 6.1.1.
6.1.2
Pile foundation
Pile foundation systems of friction piles and end-bearing piles from section 2.3.1
were used to predict certain settlement rates, for example, piles for tested frozen
soil in different temperatures. Predicted results are matched with some gathered
settlement rates from different literature research articles (Nixon and McRoberts,
1976) for frozen soil.
Settlement rates were calculated with sum equations of settlement rate for friction
pile (2.83) and settlement rate for end-bearing pile (2.90). Equations interpret
the same pile diameter as from (Nixon and McRoberts, 1976), which is 12 inches.
Based on this, a = (12 · 2.54)/2 = 15, 24cm. Values of parameters nL are derived
from Ladanyi’s model in section 5.2 and shown in table 5.2.2. Parameter BL was
never derived before. Its value is based on a log-log diagram of strain rate and
stress (5.2.1), and it is a strain rate, where stress value equals 0.001 MPa. Table
6.1.1, shows results of parameter BL for different temperatures. Values are changed
from [s−1, MPa] to [year−1, MPa].

Table 6.1.2 observes an already expected tendency that the end-bearing pile
settlement rate is almost an insignificant part of the total settlement rate. In
figure 6.1.2, predictions for different temperatures show that temperature is highly
responsible for settlement rate. Higher values of pile stress for lower temperatures
cause the same settlement rate. The tendency for -2◦C, -3◦C, -4◦C and -5◦C Celsius
are closely parallel. -10◦C UNIS EAST soil prediction has a steeper slope, resulting
in a shift where low-stress values cause similar or faster settlement rates than
warmer temperatures, and high-stress levels cause the expected tendency of high
stress for the same settlement rate. A comparison of prediction towards example
values from the literature shows that UNIS EAST soil needs a lower temperature
than the examples in the experiment. According to literature examples, predictions
have similar settlement rates between -3◦C to -5◦C. Literature examples are within
-1◦C to -3◦C (sporadically higher as Markin and Targulyan). The cause might lay
in the high salinity of UNIS EAST soil, causing high freezing point depression and
126
==PAGE:149==
CHAPTER 6. APPLICATION TO FOUNDATION DESIGN
127
samples having high unfrozen water content with warmer temperatures, causing
such predictions.
6.2
Summary
In conclusion to the foundation design application chapter, Ladanyi’s model was
picked based on its accurate long-term predictions for frozen soil. Despite neglecting
the primary state, the model showed good accuracy in section 5.2 for colder
temperatures and long-term secondary state creep deformations. The model’s
parameters were used to determine settlements and settlement rates for two
foundation systems, described in section 2.3.1. The chapter gave predictions for
the case of shallow foundations and pile foundations as a result.
Shallow foundation system prediction of the settlement was calculated, based on
Ladanyi’s parameters of bL, nL, and σcLθ, based on temperatures -3◦C and -4◦C.
The reason for choosing such temperatures was the shallow foundation’s depth and
the underground thermal regime, based on figure 4.3.2. The foundation was buried
between 2.0 to 5.0 meters in depth, corresponding to those temperatures. The rest of
the values were adopted compared to the results of article (Vyalov, 1978), which has
reported settlement of certain footing under specific load for 20 years of observation.
The article observed a circular footing of 0.705-meter diameter, under a constant
load of 0.245 MPa, embedded in plastic-frozen silty clay under 3-meter depth at a
temperature range of -0.1◦C to -1.5◦C. Settlement predictions for UNIS EAST soil
of -2◦C and -3◦C, were denoted for the same conditions of foundation and showed
along observations from an article in figure 6.1.1. Observations from Vyalov’s article
show nonlinear time-dependent deformation caused by a combination of creep
and consolidation effects. The frozen soil was warm permafrost with significant
unfrozen water content, resulting in a consolidation effect under constant load.
Consolidation evoked a strength increase, which reduced creep rate deformation.
Predictions of Ladanyi’s model for UNIS EAST soil have linear results due to the
chosen model’s assumptions and lack of consolidation inclusion. Expected results
are within the norm, showing smaller settlement, reaching 0.5 cm for -3◦C and 5.8
cm for -2◦C, after 20 years of prediction. Considering possible consolidation for
soil, nonlinear behavior would stay between ranges of settlements between these
temperatures, but only if the temperature would not have increased after 20 years
due to the climate change effect. Additional consideration of potential climate
change, based on section 2.2, reveals that after 20 years, permafrost in this area is
more likely to reach its thawing point or even lose its frozen characteristics entirely.
This might result in a more significant settlement than the one evaluated in figure
6.1.1, which could exceed the one from Vyalov’s article.
The pile foundation application in this chapter considered different approaches
towards possible settlement explorations for examined UNIS EAST soil. Prediction
used equations of 2.83 and 2.90 to calculate settlement rates for friction along
pile shaft and end-bearing effect. Settlement rates were made for similar cases of
12-inch diameter piles, similarly following observed settlement rates for certain
documented cases (Nixon and McRoberts, 1976). Settlement rates were calculated
based on different pile stresses. The method used parameters nL and BL from
127
==PAGE:150==
128
CHAPTER 6. APPLICATION TO FOUNDATION DESIGN
Ladanyi’s model. Predictions for various temperatures and researched values from
different researched documents are displayed in figure 6.1.2. Results show clear
temperature dependency of settlement rate for examined soil from UNIS EAST. An
increase in temperature results in a lower settlement rate for the same stress level.
-10◦C prediction, due to a higher slope angle, develops an expected tendency of
lower settlement rate with the same stress level only for values of stress exceeding
0.001 MPa. Compared to documented values from different sources, predictions
show that UNIS EAST soil has a higher settlement rate for similar temperatures.
Expectancy of settlement rates for Vyalov’s, Frederking’s, Huck’s, and Rowley’s
works, tested for ice-rich soils in temperatures between -1◦C to -3◦C, is the same
for UNIS EAST soil in ranges -3◦C to -5◦C. Occurring differences might be caused
by high salinity levels in an examined UNIS EAST soil, resulting in higher freezing
point depression. Considering that the thermal regime of UNIS EAST soil in the
examined location does not exceed -4◦C, it is important to consider this fact during
engineering application. The solution to that issue might be to apply artificial
cooling systems to keep settlement rates more acceptable.

==PAGE:151==
Chapter 7
Conclusions
7.1
Summary of findings
This thesis generally focused on examining permafrost frozen soil samples gathered
in the Svalbard Longyearbyen location called UNIS East, described in section 4.1.
Location conditions for air temperature, ground thermal regime, and precipitation
were examined. Annual mean temperature in Longyearbyen for 2023, was approx-
imately -5◦C with an average 0.3◦C temperature increase per decade. Annual
precipitation in the same year was denoted as 590 mm and increased yearly. Ground
temperature described in section 4.3, was concluded for three different constant
temperature levels. The mean temperature for depth of 2.0 to 5.0 meters is between
-2◦C and -3◦C, 5.0 to 12.0 meters have a mean temperature between -3◦C and -4◦C
and over 12.0 meter deep, the mean temperature stayed approximately constant
at -4◦C for the rest of measured depth (30.0 meters). Frozen soil was gathered
using an excavation process of Auger core drilling using a Geotech 505FM drilling
rig. Excavation resulted in a series of cylindrical cores for three separate boreholes,
close to each other and with depths between 2.0 and 6.6 meters, depending on the
borehole.
Examining frozen soil consisted of various tests that identified its index and
mechanical properties, which were used to classify it with standard classifications
for soils and frozen soils. Soil classification was interpreted, based on tests such as
particle size distribution with sieve and hydrometer analysis, Casagrande tests, and
cryostructure identification. Other important soil properties were also examined,
such as water content, salinity, particle density, etc. In conclusion, examined
UNIS EAST frozen soil was classified as gap-graded, glacial-alluvial silty loam
soil for a standard ISO classification based on particle size distribution, which
determined their curvatures (0.80 and 0.48), uniformity (197.69 and 97.56), and
sorting coefficient (6.58 and 5.30) for two analyzed samples. Atterberg’s limit
results concluded that the soil was low in plasticity clay (CL), with ranges of
results from four different samples for liquid limit (between 18.11% and 32.32%),
plastic limit (between 10.34% and 18.69%), plasticity index (between 7.77% and
13.63%), liquidity index (between 0.45 and 2.30) and activity (between 0.35 and
0.95). Cryostructure identified permafrost type as well-bonded with no excess ice
(Nbn) for most examined samples (17 out of 24), with visible ice inclusions as small
patches (9 out of 24). There was also one sample with ICE + soil cryostructure
type that was tested for its excess ice content due to soil oversaturation, resulting
in 69.85% excess ice with 87.31% water content. Still, it was only a small layer,
approximately 2 to 3 centimeters thick. Some samples displayed partial types
for two separate classes, which is why there are more identified cryostructures
than samples. Other tests denoted different parameters such as water content,
salinity, freezing point depression, activity, shear strength, and sensitivity, which
129
==PAGE:152==
130
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSIONS
are shown in tables 4.7.5, 4.7.7 and 4.7.12. They give averaged results of 24.43%
water content, 0.86 void ratio, 1.80 g/cm3 bulk density, 1.44 g/cm3 dry density,
2.745 g/cm3 particle density, -1.2◦C of freezing point from salinity and -1.33◦C of
freezing point from cooling curves. The appendix gives detailed parameter values
in table A.2. Section 4.7 summarizes all test results, describing examined frozen
soil in better detail. Fall cone test results gave averaged results of undisturbed
shear strength (0.92 MPa, 0.97 MPa, and 1.17 MPa), remolded shear strength
(0.61 MPa, 0.83 MPa, and 0.51 MPa), and sensitivity (1.51, 1.17 and 2.26) for
three samples accordingly.
After classification and soil parametrization, uniaxial compression tests of constant
strain rate were performed on 17 samples, studying compression strength under
different temperatures and constant strain rates. Experimental results of uniaxial
compression with constant strain rate, equal to 1.0%/min (1.67 · 10−4 s−1) in
different temperatures, resulted in compression strength equal to (2.02 MPa and
2.40 MPa) for -10◦C, (1.62 MPa and 1.94 MPa) for -5◦C, (1.24 MPa and 1.39
MPa) for -4◦C, (0.63 MPa, 0.79 MPa, 1.01 MPa and 1.01 MPa) for -3◦C and (0.42
MPa and 0.48 MPa) for -2◦C. Uniaxial compression CSR tests were also tested for
different constant strain rates with plots of the stress-strain relationship shown in
figure 4.7.11 in constant -3◦C.
Other uniaxial compression tests were performed as constant load tests, simulating
a structure placed on permafrost, resulting in time-dependent deformation caused
by the creep effect. Tests gathered information about overhead height difference and
temporary load applied at a given time. Results were notified as a series of plots,
showing the relation of load and height difference (this difference was interpreted as
the sample’s strain, which distinguishes it from typical strain definition, concluded
with Hooke’s law, Young’s modulus and stress, monitored with tensometers but
this is not possible for this case, with equipment limits described in section 4.6 and
as a result, referenced strain in time-dependent deformation is based on overhead
height difference for a given time), which were used to determine applied stress
and experienced strain, through time. Data was processed, showing results in two
sets of plots. The first strain versus time relation plots for a given constant load
are called creep curves. The second plot series shows the strain rate versus time
relation. Plots are displayed in section 4.7 for a few examples; the rest is shown
in the appendix. Plots were examined, and information about the reached creep
stage and value of minimum strain rate was denoted and shown in table 4.7.14
for different applied loads and temperatures. The applied load corresponded to
compression strength for a given constant temperature so as not to highly exceed
it or make the load too little so that the soil would barely react. Creep tests
applied loads, ranging between (0.15 kN and 0.7 kN) for -2◦C, (0.2 kN and 1.7
kN) for -3◦C, (0.6 kN and 1.7 kN) for -4◦C, (0.5 kN and 2.3 kN) for -5◦C and
(1.5 kN and 3.0 kN) for -10◦C. Time-dependent deformations varied in duration,
creep states reached, and minimum strain rates, where most results developed
either long-term secondary state creep or primary state followed by the tertiary
state. Some samples have also experienced individual long-term secondary states or
individual primary states without tertiary states at the end, especially for smaller
applied loads, compared to compression strength.
130
==PAGE:153==
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSIONS
131
Frozen soil behavior type for uniaxial compression was examined. It was based on
failure process identification after compression and independent tests of relaxation
deformation after complete unloading of compressive load. Failure processes for six
tested samples in different temperatures based on photos of compressed samples
that reached failure, shown in figure 4.7.23. Compressed samples display significant
yielding processes with deformed sides without cracking for -3◦C, yielding process
with partial cracking and buckling for -5◦C and partial yielding with considerable
cracking and buckling effect for -10◦C. Relaxation after unloading denoted elastic
strain between 0.0054 and 0.0078 and time-dependent viscous strain between 0.0031
and 0.0061 after 3 hours and between 0.0013 and 0.0034 after 3 days.
Experimental results of examined UNIS EAST frozen soil were used to choose
a constitutive model. Considering time-dependent behavior and classification of
studied samples, Ladanyi’s and Vyalov’s models were selected from six different ones
from the literature review’s section 2.1.4.2. Ladanyi’s model is proven to be effective
and accurate with ice-rich clay soils that experience long-term secondary state creep
phase with short primary state and is proven to be an accurate representation
of long-term frozen soil creep behavior in literature, frequently used in arctic
engineering, too. Vyalov’s model is designed for ice-poor clay soils and is based
on changing strain rates in the primary phase, highly developed in these soil
types. The model accurately depicts initial time-dependent creep behavior but
loses this accuracy over time, while Ladanyi’s model does the opposite. Both of
these models are a good fit for studied frozen soil, and their comparison with each
other determines that in terms of short-term time-dependent deformation, Vyalov’s
model shows a better fit with long-term time-dependent deformation, which is
crucial for engineering purposes.
Ladanyi’s and Vyalov’s model parameters were mostly denoted using graphical
methods. Their values are displaced in tables 5.2.2 and 5.3.2 respectively, and they
are of an expected range in comparison to other frozen soil’s parameters from the
literature. The results were used to create strain in time plots, depending on the
temperature, applied constant load, and an assumed instantaneous strain, and they
are displayed in section 5.4. Both models show an overall accurate time-dependent
creep behavior toward the experimental results regarding the secondary state
part. Since Ladanyi’s model neglects the primary state and assumes a certain
instantaneous strain, which includes the strain during primary state creep, it
requires adding that strain in the results at the beginning of time to display a
straight Ladanyi’s secondary state trendline for certain tests. Vyalov’s model
usually does not require this inclusion to fit the results, but for some samples,
it was necessary. The values were, however, small in comparison to Ladanyi’s
demands. However, Ladanyi’s model is more accurate for the secondary state
part than Vyalov’s. In the secondary state, the strain rate becomes more or less
constant, resulting in linear creep curves. This linear behavior is more accurate
with Ladanyi’s model towards experiments than Vyalov’s results. The frozen soil
generally experiences a short-term primary state and a long-term secondary state,
which may eventually result in a short-term tertiary state followed by failure. It is,
therefore, better to rely on the model that proves to be more accurate towards the
secondary state part than the primary when designing structures that should last a
few decades. Therefore, Ladanyi’s model is considered an accepted representation
131
==PAGE:154==
132
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSIONS
of the UNIS EAST frozen soil.
Ladanyi’s model and its denoted parameters are used for two standard foundation
systems: shallow foundations and piles for arctic engineering. Predictions are made
to test the model’s performance and observe possible UNIS EAST soil behavior
in a practical scenario. Shallow foundation case applied footing, depth, and load,
similar to (Vyalov, 1978), displaying measured settlement through 20 years for
certain circular footing in frozen soil, with temperature between -0.1◦C and -1.5◦C.
Calculations were made for -2◦C and -3◦C temperatures with different denoted
parameters for Ladanyi’s model and equation 2.72, calculating settlements for the
same footing and load. Results of settlement through time are shown in figure 6.1.1.
The non-linear behavior of Vyalov’s observations contributed to the combined effect
of time-dependent creep and consolidation effects since frozen soil’s temperatures
were close to its thawing point, resulting in an approximate 7 cm settlement after 20
years. Ladanyi’s model predictions showed linear behavior since they do not include
consolidation. Depending on temperature, predicted settlement after 20 years is
calculated between 0.18 cm and 4.6 cm for -3◦C and -2◦C respectively. If any
consolidation effect is present, the expected settlement might behave non-linearly
within the range between -2◦C and -3◦C, not accounting for climate change. It
would be an additional factor if climate change were argued and described in section
2.2. It is expected to experience partial or complete disappearance of permafrost
in this area, which could potentially increase settlement beyond -2◦C predicted
range.
Ladanyi’s model predicts pile settlement rate for friction and end-bearing piling.
(Nixon and McRoberts, 1976) is used in the comparison, which has gathered
examples of settlement rates for 12-inch piles under different stresses. It was
necessary to denote parameter BL from Ladanyi’s graphs for each temperature
and use equations 2.83 and (2.90) to calculate settlement rates for friction and
end-bearing effects of the pile foundation system. Results of predicted settlement
rates for given stress values were displayed in the plot and from other literature
documents (figure 6.1.2). As a result, predicted settlement rates for piles have
a higher value for similar temperatures as literature examples, which conclude
that UNIS EAST soil has high salinity because freezing point depression is a
lower temperature than other soils; a lower temperature is necessary to have
similar results. Overall, predictions look accurate, as do the expected behavior of
settlement rates, compared to the examples in the literature.
The thesis acknowledged constraints, mainly based on equipment limits. A com-
pression machine used for time-dependent deformation analysis could not observe
tertiary state creep properly due to the measured system, which measured the
plate’s change of uniaxial displacement over the current applied load at a given
time. The lack of tensometers caused the inability to measure stress and strain
based on Hooke’s law. The machine’s load cell was also mostly designed for ice
samples that have capacity between (0.49 kN to 9.81 kN), where the majority of
tests for -2◦C and partially for -3◦C were outside the lower limit, which makes
them potentially inaccurate. The laboratory room was also prone to temperature
instabilities that might have led to inaccurate constant temperature assessments,
potentially influencing final results.
132
==PAGE:155==
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSIONS
133
7.2
Conclusions
In conclusion, a series of standard soil tests mentioned by ISO and some additional
frozen soil tests described in the literature review successfully identified the UNIS
EAST soil from the examined location at Longyearbyen, Svalbard, for its class,
type, index, and mechanical parameters.
Two analyzed samples for particle size distribution classified examined UNIS EAST
frozen soil as silty loam based on percentiles of clay, silt, and sand fractions.
Curvature (Cc) results of less than 1 suggest poorly graded soil, and high values
of uniformity (Cu) and sorting coefficient (So) indicate gap-graded soil.
The
distribution curve shows a missing fraction between 0.1 mm and 0.3 mm particles’
diameter and suggests a transported soil of glacial-alluvial origin. The examined
soil was probably formed from an outwash deposited by meltwater from surrounding
glaciers. It is hard to say, whether permafrost is of epigenetic or syngenetic type,
but the lack of continuous deposition suggests epigenetic or at least polygenetic
type.
The denoted value of particle density based on the pycnometer method displays a
similar result to the suggested value of clay density from the literature (2.7 g/cm3).
The difference between the values might be caused by experimental inaccuracies
from weighing and material loss during testing.
The water content results (combining frozen and unfrozen water content) suggest a
moderate amount of water present in the soil. This amount is significant enough to
create a considerable ice cementation effect upon lower negative temperatures, where
frozen water content is much higher than unfrozen. The closer the temperature
to its thawing point, the more water content becomes unfrozen, causing ice-soil
cementation loss and increasing saturation. Both of these effects work unfavorably
for the soil’s strength.
Values of void ratio, bulk density, and dry density suggest a compacted, fairly
dense soil with fewer voids, with or without the frozen aspect of examined UNIS
EAST soil.
Freezing point depression was calculated in two ways: with salinity and cooling
curves. They both display similar results for the same samples, enhancing their
accuracy towards reality. Salinity tests recorded values along three boreholes. One
borehole observed a higher salinity level at a 5.5-meter depth with an almost 3.5%
value, where typically the value was between 1.5% and 2.5%. Higher salinity at
that level might correlate with the ocean’s level as the examined area is fairly close
to salty Arctic ocean water.
Atterberg limit tests determined the soil to be a low-plasticity clay (CL). In
contrast, its activity varies from inactive to normal, depending on the used clay’s
fraction determination of two tested samples for particle size distribution. Inactive
to normal activity suggests a moderate volume change caused by moisture change.
The results of Atterberg limits are made on thawed samples, so this activity does
not apply to frozen soil. Still, if the soil has thawed, activity displays a moderate
volume change upon moisture variation.
133
==PAGE:156==
134
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSIONS
Cryostructure identification concludes that frozen soil is mostly insusceptible to
the slightly susceptible matrix for unpredictable creep behavior. Well-bonded ice
with unsaturated soil has a largely uniform frozen soil matrix. The more uniform
the matrix, the less unpredictable it behaves. Randomly placed large ice patches
or thick ice layers followed by weaker soil layers can behave unpredictably, which
is not desired in engineering.
Fall cone test results for three samples concluded certain shear strength values for
undisturbed, thawed, and remolded samples. Undisturbed and remolded strengths
have a 100 times higher shear strength than thawed ones. The cause of this
difference lies in the test procedure, which uses cone penetration that delves into
the soil, and depth penetration is correlated with strength. The test seems to be
designed for unfrozen soil because ice cementation hardens the sample, and cone
penetration is very limited, although present. However, sensitivity results suggest
a low-sensitive soil, which is fairly common for frozen soil, as cohesion is greatly
decreased, resulting in higher shear strength.
Uniaxial compression test results of constant strain rate equal to 1.0%/min (1.67 ·
10−4 s−1), for different temperatures show high differences for compression strength
concerning temperature. The difference between the lowest value at the highest
temperature of -2◦C is six times smaller than the strength of the lowest temperature
of -10◦C. The highest strength loss is shown between -3◦C and -2◦C which makes
sense, as these temperatures are the closest to the soil’s thawing point. Other
uniaxial compression CSR tests subjected to different constant strain rates in the
same temperature of -3◦C showed that strain rate influences the size of peak stress
before failure and the strain’s size upon failure. A higher strain rate decreases
failing strain, probably because ductile behavior has no time to develop, and
therefore, the sample fails more abruptly in a brittle process. High strain rate also
results in lower peak stress, because of the abrupt end of the strain process. Lower
strain rates have a higher tendency to experience failing strain at higher values,
but they decrease peak stress even more than high strain rates. Longer ductile
deformation during compression allows for greater strain, but peak stress is not
high.
Uniaxial compression test results of constant load tests for different temperatures
and loads have experienced a variety of time-dependent deformations. The thermal
regime limited the range of applied load so it would not result in short-term
failure or insignificant deformation. In contrast to compression strength for a
given temperature, lower and medium loads applied tended to develop long-term
secondary states that do not fail (at least for a moment after stopping the test).
Sometimes, creep would not exceed beyond the primary state as well. Higher
applied loads mostly ended on a long-term secondary state that ended with a
tertiary state or an immediate change from the primary to tertiary state. The lower
the temperature, the more likely the soil would develop a long-term secondary
creep state. All of this proves temperature dependency and that frozen soil is
most likely to behave like ice if the applied load is a reasonable amount toward the
compressive strength of frozen soil.
The failure process identified by studying the compressed samples has observed
ductile behavior of frozen soil for temperature -3circC and ductile/brittle transition
134
==PAGE:157==
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSIONS
135
behavior for -5circC and- 10circC. These observations suggest more significant
viscoplastic behavior of compressed soil rather than elastic. Relaxation results
confirm this: elastic relaxation is below 0.01 of the whole strain. Relaxation
results also denote a time-dependent relaxation for the viscous part of the unloaded
sample.
Results of uniaxial compression constant load tests (creep tests) were used both
for model comparison and model selection. They were deemed mostly accurate
for Ladanyi’s model in colder frozen silty soil with dominant secondary state
creep and clear primary state creep present and Vyalov’s model for warmer frozen
silty soil with longer primary state creep development. Tertiary state creep was
neglected due to established constraints of inaccurate load assessment for used
equipment. Results of both models show an overall accurate and similar behavior
for experimental creep test results. Still, one model is more precise in a more
relevant part of time-dependent deformation. Ladanyi’s model focuses on secondary
state creep, neglecting the primary state completely, and demands to include certain
instantaneous strain with primary state strain included, but strain prediction in
secondary state creep, which is a dominant part of time-dependent behavior and is
more accurate than Vyalov’s model considers both primary and secondary states.
This observation gives Ladanyi’s model an advantage over Vyalov’s model, as
secondary state creep is the one that should be focused on in arctic geotechnical
engineering because predictions are made for several decades and based on literature
and experimental results of UNIS EAST soil, the behavior of frozen soil is divided
into short-term primary state, followed by long-term secondary state, followed
by the short-term tertiary state that eventually leads to failure. It is, therefore,
practical to design solely for secondary state creep. This conclusion corresponds
with the objective of investigating UNIS EAST frozen soil and determining a
suitable constitutive model for an accurate long-term time-dependent deformation
simulation (Objective is explained in detail, in section 3.1) and answers it with
Ladanyi’s model being chosen as an accurate representation of time-dependent
examined UNIS EAST frozen soil expected and examined behavior for practical
designing that is performed for long-term time-dependent behavior of frozen soil.
The conclusions of compression strength results, model equations, parameter
values, experimental creep curves, and failure process correspond with the thesis’s
objective of proving and observing frozen soil’s temperature dependence (Objective
is explained in section 3.1). Temperature plays an enormous role in the mechanical
properties of frozen soil because frozen water content adds significant strength
to soil material. The bigger the frozen water content over the unfrozen one, the
higher the strength and behavior of frozen soil, which behaves similarly to ice.
Foundation predictions are based on Ladanyi’s model, chosen over Vyalov’s model
for its higher accuracy towards long-term predictions with dominant secondary
state creep of time-dependent deformation in frozen UNIS EAST soil. Predictions
for shallow foundation and pile systems, common for Arctic engineering, show
a good comparison between observed and measured settlements and settlement
rates for real-life examples from the literature. Expected values suggest that
UNIS EAST frozen soil has a lower freezing point depression than other frozen
soils from the literature due to its high salinity. The examined location is on an
135
==PAGE:158==
136
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSIONS
island in the middle of the Arctic Ocean with a high salinity value. Predicted
settlement for applied stresses on certain foundations, show expected tendencies
and values and be accurate towards overall engineering geotechnical assessment.
This conclusion corresponds with the objective of predicting foundation settlement
and settlement rates based on a chosen model compared to real observations of
foundation deformation from other scientific sources (Objective is explained in
detail in section 3.1). The thesis picks Ladanyi’s model for foundation predictions,
which essentially shows the model’s effectiveness and compares values to some
real-life examples that do not differ greatly from each other and might be considered
plausible.
7.3
Discussion and future work
In retrospect to the overall effect and conclusions of this thesis, there is some
potential improvement that could have been made during the experimental process.
Many potential inaccuracies came from used equipment primarily designed for ice,
not frozen soil. This would not have been an issue for colder frozen soil, which
has a temperature nowhere close to its thawing point and behaves similarly to
ice in time-dependent deformation upon compression. Still, this work was done
partially for temperatures close to the soil’s thawing point, which exceeded the load
cell’s capacity with its lower limit. The experimental uniaxial compression testing
should have been performed either on higher loads or the load cell should have
been changed to the one with a capacity within applied loads by the compression
machine.
It could also be argued that the number of creep tests could have been reduced
and lengthened in time for individual tests. Time-dependent deformation analysis
should be performed in the long term, lasting a couple of days, if not weeks. Creep
tests in this thesis rarely exceeded 24 hours of test duration, and some tests were
stopped manually during compression for their low strain rate.
Although this thesis’s results were concluded to be accurate, they are based on either
experimental laboratory results made for this thesis or individual examples from
other literature that assess time-dependent behavior and foundation settlements
for other frozen soils. There is a lot of potential improvement here for testing the
accuracy of Ladanyi’s model, which was chosen as the most accurate depiction
of long-term time-dependent deformation of UNIS EAST frozen soil. Literature
sources of foundations’ measured settlements and settlement rates over a long
period confirm expected predictions.
Even so, although Ladanyi’s model shows good accuracy, it was necessary to predict
instantaneous strain using experimental results because of primary state negligence.
Future work exploring instantaneous and primary state strain is needed in this case.
Results denoted an instantaneous strain ranging between 5% to 15% of overall
experienced strain, depending on temperature and applied load. To accurately
depict Ladanyi’s model, additional exploration of that part should be made as an
accurate depiction of frozen soil’s behavior from Ladanyi’s.
Compared to built and built real cases, additional tests and calculations for
136
==PAGE:159==
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUSIONS
137
Ladanyi’s model are a good future work addition to verify the model. The purpose
of choosing a constitutive model for examined frozen soil was to depict an expected
long-term time-dependent deformation behavior for UNIS EAST frozen soil for
engineering purposes. If the model does not assess accurate results for actual
engineering cases, it needs corrections in parametrization or should be changed to
a different model. The model’s parameters in this thesis are based on a few test
results in different temperatures. Although assessment looks expected for ice-rich
frozen soil, it may not be enough to use it with high certainty for engineering
purposes. Therefore, additional Ladanyi’s model examination is suggested in
practice.
The conclusion of the secondary objective deduced that permafrost and frozen soil,
in general, are highly influenced by temperature because most of their strength
comes from frozen water content that increases cohesion due to ice cementation.
The biggest difference in strength is present, close to the thawing temperature of
frozen soil, influenced by salinity. Exploration of both the ground thermal regime
of the UNIS EAST location and the freezing point depression of frozen soil is
only different by a couple of degrees. With an additional expectancy of increasing
temperature due to climate change, the situation does not look favorable towards
an overall frozen soil’s strength performance throughout the next decades. In
future work, examining climate change’s influence on frozen ground temperatures
should be greater because using a frozen aspect of permafrost as an advantage
highly relies on its temperature.
Other future work suggestions rely on passive and active artificial cooling systems.
For a given conclusion that climate change influences permafrost and leads to its
partial or complete disappearance, additional future work towards active artificial
cooling systems for frozen soil is mandatory if engineering design in the Arctic still
uses frozen soil as a geotechnical approach in design. Climate change influence,
concerning temperature dependency and the small difference between the thermal
in-situ regime and freezing point depression, might conclude that active artificial
cooling would be too expensive or impossible to maintain to effectively utilize
frozen soil’s behavior with increased temperature over the next decades. That
is why future work exploring artificial cooling systems’ effectiveness for climate
change effects should be considered.
137
